Qasamah for a Body Found Between Two Villages
Ruling on qasamah when body is between two villages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for qasamah when a body is found between two villages, identifying the liable community based on proximity and citing the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Responsibility for qasamah falls on the nearer village, even by a handspan.

When a dead body is discovered between two settlements, Islamic law must determine which community is responsible for the collective oath (qasamah) and blood money (diyah). The Hanafi school, following the Prophet's guidance, sets a clear physical criterion: proximity. This lesson examines the ruling and its evidence from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
A body is found between two villages. The ruling from the matn is that the nearer village (al-aqrab) bears the responsibility for qasamah and diyah.
The ruling is categorical—only the closer village is liable.
2
Understand the criterion: proximity
The determining factor is physical closeness. The sharh explains that we see which village is nearer in distance; only that village is obligated.
Even a small difference suffices, as shown in the hadith.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The commentator cites a hadith from Abu Sa'id: a murdered person was found between two tribes, and the Prophet ordered measurement. It was found closer to one tribe by a handspan, and the Prophet assigned the blood money to that tribe.
This shows that the Prophet himself established proximity as the sole criterion.
4
Apply the principle
In practice, one must measure the distance from the body to each village. Whichever is closer—even by a handspan—is liable. No proportionality or sharing is considered.
If exactly equidistant, the matn does not specify; but the hadith suggests measurement to resolve it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۴۲۴] (۱۸) وان وجد بين قريتين كان على اقربهما.
(ويليه شرح) اگر مقتول دوگاؤں کے درمیان پایا جائے تو دونوں گاؤں کے قریب والوں پر دیت ہوگی۔ مقتول دو گاؤں کے درمیان پڑا ہوا ملا تو دیکھا جائے گا کہ کس گاؤں سے وہ زیادہ قریب ہے اسی گاؤں والوں پر قسامت اور دیت لازم ہوگی۔
(وتتمة) قریب والے پر ہی لازم کیا جا سکتا ہے اور کیا کریں (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابی سعید ان قتيلا وجد بين حيين فامر النبي الله ان يقاس الى ايهما اقرب فوجد اقرب الى احد الحيين بشبر قال ابو سعيد كأني انظر الى شبر رسول الله الله القفی دیته عليهم (و) (سنن بیہقی ، باب ماروی فی العقیل یوجد بین الحسین ج خامس ص ۲۱۷، نمبر۱۶۴۵۳) اس حدیث اور اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ ایک بالشت بھی قریب ہو تو اس پر قسامت ہوگی۔
(18) And if he is found between two villages, it (the responsibility) is upon the closer one. Commentary: If the murdered person is found between two villages, the blood money is upon the people of the closer village. When the body is found between two villages, it will be seen which village is closer; that village's people are obligated for qasamah and blood money. (Continuation) It can only be imposed on the nearer one. And what should they do? (2) There is a hadith: from Abu Sa'id that a murdered person was found between two tribes, and the Prophet ordered that it be measured to which one it was closer. It was found closer to one tribe by a handspan. Abu Sa'id said: As if I am looking at the handspan of the Messenger of Allah. He assigned the blood money to them. (d) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab ma Ruwiya fi al-'Aqil Yujad bayn al-Husayn, vol. 5, p. 217, no. 16453) From this hadith and precedent it is understood that even if it is closer by a handspan, qasamah applies.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This final ruling addresses a body found between two villages. The matn states that responsibility falls on the nearer village. The sharh explains that we determine which village is closer geographically; that village's residents must perform qasamah and pay the diyah. The commentator supports this with a hadith in which the Prophet measured the distance and assigned responsibility to the closer village even by a handspan. Thus, even a slight proximity triggers liability. The ruling is based on the principle that the closer village is more likely to be involved or have knowledge of the murder. This establishes a clear methodology: physical proximity determines the liable group.

Key Terms
القَرْيَةُ (al-qaryah): A village or large settlement.
الأَقْرَبُ (al-aqrab): The nearer in distance; determines which community is responsible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a body is found exactly midway between two villages, what should be done according to the hadith?
A) Both villages share the blood money equally
B) No village is responsible; blood money from treasury
C) Measure the distance to determine the closer village
D) The larger village takes responsibility

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines qasamah liability based on physical proximity, even a slight difference, as proven by the Prophet's practice.

Ruling When Specifically Accused by Guardian
Ruling when the guardian accuses a specific person from the locality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a specific accusation by the guardian does not lift qasamah from the locality and explain the legal reasoning from the hadith.

Core Idea
A specific accusation does not drop qasamah; the community remains obligated to cooperate.

In cases of unwitnessed murder, the Hanafi school imposes a collective oath (qasamah) on the locality where the body is found. But what if the guardian points at one specific individual? Does that exempt the rest? This lesson examines the ruling from the matn and the supporting hadith, clarifying that the community's duty remains intact to prevent evasion of justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
Read the matn statement: 'If the guardian accuses a specific man from the people of the locality, the qasamah is not dropped from the people of the locality.' This is the core ruling.
The ruling is an exception to the assumption that accusation shifts liability.
2
Examine the condition
The condition is that the wali accuses a specific person but without proof. Even with such an accusation, the obligation on the locality remains.
The wali must be a legitimate guardian; the accusation alone is insufficient to remove collective responsibility.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith of Sahl ibn Abi Hathma shows a similar scenario: the family accused specific Jews, but no evidence. The Prophet offered to have the accused swear oaths, but the family refused, so he paid diyah from public funds. This indicates that the community (locality) is involved.
The hadith is ahad (zanni thubut) but accepted for establishing rulings in qasamah.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The legal implication: Since the Prophet did not drop the case but instead sought oaths from the accused and ultimately paid diyah, it shows that a specific accusation without proof does not exonerate the community. The qasamah mechanism remains to compel the locality to either reveal the killer or swear oaths.
The Hanafi position prioritizes blood not being wasted over accepting an unsubstantiated accusation.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani held that if the wali has strong suspicion, the locality may be exempt. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is that of Abu Hanifa: qasamah remains on the locality.
This ikhtilaf shows the spectrum of ijtihad within the school; the majority and the matn uphold the stricter view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ادعى الولي على رجل بعينه من أهل المحلة لم تسقط القسامة عن أهل المحلة. عن سهل بن أبي حثمة أن نفرا من قومه انطلقوا إلى خيبر فتفرقوا فيها فوجدوا أحدهم قتيلا فقالوا للذين وجدوه عندهم قتلتم صاحبنا فقالوا ما قتلناه ولا علمنا قاتلا فانطلقوا إلى نبي الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال لهم تأتوني بالبينة على من قتل هذا قالوا ما لنا بينة قال فيحلفون لكم قالوا لا نرضى بأيمان اليهود فكره رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يطل دمه فوداه مائة من إبل الصدقة.
And if the guardian accuses a specific man from the people of the locality, the qasamah is not dropped from the people of the locality. On the authority of Sahl ibn Abi Hathma: A group of his people went to Khaybar and dispersed there. They found one of them killed. They said to those who found him near them, 'You killed our companion.' They replied, 'We did not kill him, nor do we know the killer.' So they went to the Prophet of Allah (peace be upon him). He said to them, 'Will you bring me evidence of who killed him?' They said, 'We have no evidence.' He said, 'Then they will take oaths for you.' They said, 'We are not satisfied with the oaths of the Jews.' The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) disliked that his blood be wasted, so he paid a hundred camels from the charity camels as blood money.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where the guardian (wali) of the slain person points to a specific individual from the locality as the murderer, but without evidence. The matn states that such an accusation does not lift the qasamah obligation from the entire locality. The hadith of Sahl ibn Abi Hathma illustrates this: the companions of the slain man accused the people of Khaybar, but they had no proof. The Prophet offered to have the accused take oaths, but the family refused to accept the oaths of Jews. Therefore, the Prophet paid the diyah from public funds to avoid wasting blood. This shows that when the guardian names a specific person but cannot prove it, qasamah shifts to the community to encourage them to either reveal the killer or swear oaths. If they refuse oaths, they become liable. However, in the hadith, since the accused were Jews and the family refused their oaths, the Prophet paid diyah to prevent blood being wasted.

Key Terms
ولي (wali): Guardian of the slain, typically the closest male relative entitled to claim blood money or retaliation.
قسامة (qasamah): Collective oath-taking by the locality when a murdered person is found without evidence, to either swear innocence or become liable for diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the guardian of a slain person accuses a specific individual from a locality but provides no evidence, what is the Hanafi ruling on qasamah?
A) Qasamah is dropped from the locality and applies only to the accused.
B) Qasamah remains on the locality; the accusation does not exempt them.
C) The accused must pay diyah immediately.
D) The case is dismissed entirely.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a specific accusation by the guardian does not lift the qasamah obligation from the locality; the community must still swear oaths or pay diyah.

Corpse in Flowing Water: The Ruling of H
Ruling on liability when corpse is found in flowing water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a corpse found in flowing water is هدر (no liability) and explain the rationale of uncertainty.

Core Idea
A corpse in flowing water is هدر — uncertainty of origin bars liability.

This passage from the Qasamah chapter addresses a practical scenario: a dead body found in a flowing river. The key issue is whether any party can be held liable for the death. The Hanafi ruling directly addresses the role of uncertainty in criminal liability, establishing that when the origin is unknown, no one is required to pay compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Note the specific conditions: a corpse found in the middle of a flowing river like the Euphrates, with water continuously passing over it.
This is distinct from a corpse found in a well or a closed area.
2
State the Ruling
The text declares 'فهو هدر' — it is wasted, meaning no blood money (diya) is due.
The term 'هدر' is a legal designation for forfeited blood.
3
Understand the Rationale
Because the water flows over the body, its origin is unknown. Therefore, no specific locality can be blamed.
Uncertainty (جهالة) prevents establishing liability.
4
Apply the Legal Concept
Recognize that this ruling protects communities from unfounded accusations when the killer cannot be identified.
This is a manifestation of the principle that liability requires certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان وجد في وسط الفرات يمر بها الماء فهو هدر (٢٤٢٥) (۱۹) اگر فرات ندی کے درمیان پایا گیا جس کو پانی بہالے جار ہا ہو تو خون رائیگاں ہے۔ فرات ندی کے درمیان لاش ہے اور پانی اس کو بہالے جارہا ہے تو وہ لاش کہاں سے آرہی ہے اس کا پتا نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کسی محلے والے کو مجرم قرار نہیں دیا جا سکتا۔ اس لئے اس کا خون معاف ہے (۲) اثر پہلے گزر چکا ہے۔
And if found in the middle of the Euphrates with water flowing over it, it is wasted. If found in the middle of the Euphrates, carried by water, then the blood is forfeited. The corpse is in the middle of the Euphrates and water is flowing over it, so it is not known where the corpse came from. Therefore no locality can be held responsible. So its blood is excused. (2) The effect has passed previously.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the chapter on Qasamah (oath-taking in cases of found dead bodies). The ruling addresses a scenario where a corpse is found in the middle of a flowing river like the Euphrates, with water continuously passing over it. Since the origin of the corpse is unknown, no particular locality can be blamed. The Hanafi ruling is that such a case is considered هدر (hadar), meaning the blood is wasted and no compensation is required. The commentary in Urdu explains this: the water flows over the body, so it is unclear where it came from; therefore no one is deemed a criminal, and the blood is forfeited. The note "(2) اثر پہلے گزر چکا ہے" refers to a hadith that has already been mentioned in an earlier part of the book, which likely established the principle that a corpse in flowing water carries no liability.

Key Terms
هدر (hadar): Blood that is forfeited, meaning no liability for compensation arises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when a corpse is found in the middle of a flowing river like the Euphrates, what is the ruling?
A) The nearest village must pay blood money
B) It is هدر, and no compensation is due
C) The person who last saw the victim is responsible
D) The river owner is liable

Key Takeaway
When a corpse is found in flowing water, Hanafi law deems the blood هدر, meaning no one is liable for compensation due to the uncertainty of the corpse's origin.

Qasamah for a Body at the Riverbank: Liability of the Nearest Village
Ruling when corpse is stopped at the riverbank

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qasamah when a corpse is stopped at a riverbank and identify the hadith evidence for determining the responsible village.

Core Idea
A corpse stopped at the bank points liability to the nearest village for qasamah.

In the Hanafi school, qasamah (oath-taking) is a mechanism to establish or deny liability when a murdered body is found near a locality. This lesson focuses on a specific scenario: the body is found at a riverbank, stationary, with apparent evidence of murder. The ruling hinges on proximity, not on the location of discovery alone. Understanding this rule helps you see how the law uses circumstantial evidence (qarā'in) to assign responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The corpse is at the riverbank, stopped (not floating). It is stationary, and there is apparent evidence (qareenah) that someone from the nearest village killed him and threw him in.
Distinguish from a body found in the middle of flowing water, which is treated as hādir (no liability).
2
State the ruling
The nearest village must take qasamah – fifty oaths – swearing they did not commit the murder. If they refuse, they must pay blood money (diyah).
Qasamah is wājib upon them, not merely recommended.
3
Understand the rationale
The apparent sign (qareenah) of the body at the bank indicates that the killing was done by someone from the closest settlement. This circumstantial evidence is given weight.
This is based on the hadith where the Prophet ﷺ ordered measurement of proximity.
4
Memorize the hadith proof
The hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: a murdered person was found between two settlements; the Prophet ﷺ ordered to measure which one was closer, and the oath was upon that village.
Collected by al-Bayhaqi. The 'amr (command) indicates obligation in Hanafi uṣūl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان كان محتبسا بالشاطئ فهو على اقرب القرى من ذلك المكان (۲۴۲٦) (۲۰) اور اگر رکا ہوا ہو کنارے پر تو قسامت قریب والے گاؤں پر ہوگی۔ لاش فرات ندی کے کنارے پر رکی ہوئی ہے اور اندازہ ہوتا ہے کہ قریب کے محلے والے نے مار کر ندی میں ڈال دیا ہے تو پھر چونکہ ظاہری علامت قریب محلے والے کے قتل کی ہے اس لئے قریب کے محلے والے پر قسامت ہوگی۔ او پر حدیث گزری کہ جو گاؤں قریب ہو ان پر قسامت ہوگی ۔ عن ابی سعید ان قتيلا وجد بين حيين فامر النبي الله ان يقاس الى ايهما اقرب (الف) (سن للبیہقی ، باب ماروی فی التقتيل يوجد بین قریتین ولا صبح ج مامن ص ۲۱۷، نمبر ۱۶۴۵٣)
And if it was stopped at the bank, then it is upon the nearest village from that place. And if it was stopped at the bank, then qasamah will be upon the nearest village. The corpse is stopped at the bank of the Euphrates, and it is estimated that someone from the nearest locality killed him and threw him into the river. So because the apparent sign indicates murder by the nearest locality, qasamah will be upon the nearest locality. And a hadith has passed that the village which is nearest, qasamah will be upon them. From Abu Sa'id that a murdered person was found between two settlements, so the Prophet ﷺ ordered that it be measured to which one it was closer. (Al-Bayhaqi, Bab ma ruwiyya fi al-qatil yujadu bayna qaryatayn...)
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on Qasamah. When a corpse is found at the riverbank (as opposed to the middle of the flowing water), the apparent evidence points to the nearest village. Therefore, the Hanafi ruling is that qasamah (oath-taking) is required from the residents of that nearest village to swear that they did not kill him. The Urdu commentary explains: because the body is stopped at the bank, it is suspected that someone from the nearby locality killed him and disposed of the body. The ruling is supported by a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, where the Prophet ﷺ instructed that if a body is found between two settlements, measure which one is closer, and the oath is upon that village. This hadith is referenced from Sunan al-Bayhaqi.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasamah): Oaths taken by the residents of a locality to deny responsibility for a murder found in their vicinity.
قريبة (qareenah): Apparent evidence or circumstantial sign that points to the likely perpetrator or location.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when a corpse is found at the bank of a river?
A) It is هدر like the middle of the river
B) The nearest village must take qasamah
C) The government is responsible
D) The person who owns the bank is liable

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that when a murdered corpse is stopped at a riverbank, the nearest village must perform qasamah (oaths) to deny liability, and if they refuse, they pay blood money.

6 min
Qasamah Not Dropped by Accusation of Specific Person
Ruling on qasamah when a specific person is accused

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that qasamah remains wajib on the locality when a specific person is accused without proof, and explain the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Accusing a specific person without proof does not remove collective qasamah; the locality still swears.

In cases of murder where the killer is unknown, Hanafi law imposes qasamah (collective oaths) on the locality to preserve the victim's right to justice. This lesson examines a specific scenario: the heir points to a particular individual from the same locality as the killer, but lacks witnesses or conclusive proof. The rule is clear—this does not absolve the locality of its collective responsibility. The passage explains the reasoning and cites a prophetic hadith to reinforce the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the first sentence: 'And if the heir claims against one specific person from the people of the locality, qasamah is not dropped from them.' This is the main hukm.
The Arabic phrase 'لم تسقط القسامة عنهم' is emphatic.
2
Understand the rationale
The explanation states that because the accusation is unproven, letting the matter drop would waste the victim's blood. Thus collective responsibility remains to ensure the blood is not wasted.
Hanafi law prioritizes protection of life over procedural gaps.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
The passage quotes the hadith of Sahl ibn Abi Hathma: the Prophet ﷺ asked for witnesses, and when none were provided, he said 'Then they (the people of Khaybar) can swear oaths for you.' This shows that the locality is required to swear even when a specific accusation is made but not proved.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawood no. 4523, with zanni thubut.
4
Apply the ruling to a parallel scenario
Consider a body found in a neighbourhood; the heir names a suspect but has no proof. According to this ruling, the whole neighbourhood must swear 50 oaths. If they refuse or fail, they collectively pay diyah (blood money).
This prevents the victim's rights from being nullified by an unsubstantiated allegation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ادعی الولى على واحد من اهل المحلة بعينه لم تسقط القسامة عنهم (۲٤۲۷) (۲۱) اگر ولی نے محلے والے میں سے کسی ایک مخصوص پر قتل کا دعوی کیا تب بھی محلے والے سے قسامت ساقط نہیں ہوگی۔ ان مقتول کے ولی نے دعوی کیا کہ محلہ کے فلاں آدمی نے اس کو قتل کیا ہے۔ لیکن اس پر کوئی بینہ اور گواہ نہیں ہے صرف گمان غالب ہے اس لئے خاص آدمی پرقتل کا دعوی ثابت نہیں ہوگا۔ اب یوں چھوڑ دیں تو اس کا خون بیکار جائے گا اس لئے محلہ والوں سے قسم لیکر ان پر دیت لازم ہوگی۔
حدیث میں ہے کہ انصار کے کچھ لوگ خیبر گئے۔ ان میں سے ایک کو قتل کر دیا تو اس کے ولی نے حضور کے سامنے شکایت کی کہ فلاں نے قتل کیا ہے۔ آپ نے پوچھا اس پر گواہ ہے؟ فرمایا نہیں ! تو آپ نے فرمایا اہل خیبر سے قسم لے سکتے ہو۔ حدیث یہ ہے۔ سهل بن ابنی حشمة اخبره ان نفرا من قومه انطلقوا الى خيبر فتفرقوا فيها فوجدوا احدهم قتيلا فقالوا للذين وجدوه عندهم قتلتم صاحبنا؟ فقالوا ما قتلناه ولا علمنا قاتلا فانطلقنا الى نبى الله الله الله قال فقال لهم تأتوني بالبينة على من قتل هذا؟ قالوا مالنا بيئة قال فيحلفون لكم (ب) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی ترک القود بالقسامة ص ۲۷۴ نمبر ۴۵۲۳) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ ایک مخصوص آدمی پر دعوی ہو لیکن گواہ کے ذریعہ ثابت نہ کر سکے تو محلے والے پر قسامت ہوگی تاکہ خون باطل نہ جائے۔ حاشیہ : (پچھلے صفحہ سے آگے ) فرماتے ہیں کہ میں حضور کے بالشت کو گویا کہ دیکھ رہا ہوں تو آپ نے انہیں لوگوں پر اس کی دیت ڈال دی (الف) حضرت ابوسعید فرماتے ہیں کہ ایک مقتول کو دو گاؤں کے درمیان پایا تو آپ نے قیاس کرنے کے لئے کہا کہ کس کے زیادہ قریب ہے (ب) سهل بن ابی حمہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس کی قوم کے کچھ لوگ خیبر گئے وہاں ادھر ادھر پھیل گئے تو ان میں سے ایک کو مقتول پایا۔ جن کے پاس مقتول ملے ان سے کہا کہ تم نے ہمارے لوگوں کو قتل کیا ہے؟ ان لوگوں نے کہا کہ نہ ہم نے قتل کیا ہے اور نہ ہم قاتل کو جانتے ہیں۔ پھر ہم حضور کے پاس گئے تو آپ نے فرمایا۔ کس نے قتل کیا اس پر گواہ لاؤ! ان حضرات نے فرمایا ہمارے پاس گواہ نہیں ہے۔ آپ نے فرمایا تمہارے لئے قسمیں کھا ئیں۔
And if the heir claims against one specific person from the people of the locality, qasamah is not dropped from them. (21) If the heir claimed murder against a specific person from the locality, even then qasamah will not be dropped from the locality. The heir of the victim claimed that a certain man from the locality killed him, but he has no clear evidence or witnesses, only a strong suspicion, so the murder claim against that specific person is not proven. Now if we leave it, his blood will go to waste, so oaths are taken from the locality and blood money becomes obligatory upon them. There is a hadith that some people from the Ansar went to Khaybar... [the hadith is quoted] From this hadith it is understood that if a claim is made against a specific person but cannot be proven by witnesses, then qasamah is upon the locality so that the blood is not wasted. Footnote: (continuing from previous page) He said, 'I can almost see the handspan of the Prophet, and he placed the blood money upon those people.' (a) Abu Sa'id said that a murdered person was found between two villages, and the Prophet ordered that it be measured to which it was closer. (b) Sahl ibn Abi Hathma said... [the hadith repeated]
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter on Qasamah. The situation: the heir (wali) of the victim accuses a specific individual from the same locality of the murder. However, he lacks witnesses or conclusive proof. The Hanafi rule: the qasamah is not dropped from the entire locality; they must still swear oaths collectively. The reason is that if the specific accusation is not provable, letting the matter go would waste the victim's blood. Therefore, to protect life, the collective responsibility remains. The text then cites a hadith from Sahl ibn Abi Hathma about some Ansar who went to Khaybar; one was found dead. The Prophet ﷺ asked for witnesses, and when none were available, he said 'Then they (the people of Khaybar) can swear oaths for you.' This shows that when no witness is present, the locality (here, Khaybar) is required to take oaths. The footnote adds a hadith about measuring proximity between two villages, reinforcing the principle of nearest locality. The hashiah also mentions the Prophet's handspan, likely from a different narration. In summary, the ruling ensures that victim's rights are not forfeited due to lack of evidence against an individual; the locality as a whole is still subject to qasamah.

Key Terms
ولي (wali): The legal guardian or heir of the victim who has the right to prosecute or claim blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It remains on the locality because the accusation is unproven
C) The accused specific person alone must swear oaths
D) The qasamah is transferred to the deceased's relatives

Key Takeaway
Qasamah on a locality is not lifted by an unproven accusation against a specific person; collective responsibility ensures the victim's blood is not wasted.

5 min
Claim Against an Outsider Drops Qasamah from Locality
Ruling when heir accuses someone outside the locality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that qasamah drops from a locality when the heir accuses an outsider, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
Accusing an outsider exonerates the locality from qasamah.

In the chapter on Qasamah (oaths in homicide cases), Hanafi fiqh establishes specific rules for collective responsibility. This lesson presents a key ruling: if the heir of the victim accuses a specific person outside the locality where the body was found, the obligation of qasamah falls from the residents of that locality. The reasoning is that the accusation shifts suspicion away from the locality, so there is no basis to require oaths from them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The ruling applies when the heir (wali) makes a claim (da'wa) against a specific person who is not from the locality (ghayrihim).
The accused must be an outsider – not just unknown.
2
Recognize the consequence
Once the condition is met, the qasamah obligation falls (saqata) from the entire locality. No oaths are required from the residents.
Saqata means 'dropped' or 'removed'.
3
Understand the rationale
By accusing an outsider, the heir indicates that the locality is not involved in the murder. Hence, there is no basis for collective responsibility.
4
Distinguish from the insider case
If the heir accuses someone from within the locality, qasamah remains. The insider accusation does not exonerate the community.
Contrast with the previous ruling.
5
Note the practical effect
The locality is free from qasamah, but the accused outsider may still face prosecution if sufficient evidence exists outside the qasamah framework.
Qasamah is only one legal avenue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ادعى على واحد من غيرهم سقطت عنهم (۲٤۲۸) (۲۲) اور اگر محلے کے علاوہ میں سے کسی پر دعوی ہو تو محلے والے سے ساقط ہو جائے گی۔ جب محلے کے علاوہ آدمی پر قتل کا دعوی ہوا تو معلوم ہوا کہ محلے والے اس میں ملوث نہیں ہیں۔ اس لئے محلے والوں سے قسامت ساقط ہو جائے گی۔
And if he claims against one from other than them, it falls from them. (22) And if the claim is against someone outside the locality, then qasamah will be dropped from the locality. When the murder claim is against a person outside the locality, it becomes evident that the locality is not involved. Therefore qasamah will be dropped from the locality.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on Qasamah. The rule is straightforward: if the heir (wali) accuses a specific person who is not from the locality where the body was found, then the qasamah is dropped from that locality. The reasoning given in the Urdu commentary is that by accusing someone outside, the heir indicates that the locality is not implicated in the murder. Therefore, there is no need to require oaths from them. The ruling contrasts with the previous one where accusing an insider did not drop qasamah. The key difference is the perception of involvement: an outsider accusation exonerates the locality, while an insider accusation still leaves doubt, so collective responsibility remains. This ensures that blood is not wasted while avoiding burdening innocent people.

Key Terms
سقط (saqata): To fall or become dropped; here it means the obligation of qasamah is removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It remains on the locality
B) It is dropped from the locality
C) The outsider must take qasamah alone
D) The qasamah is doubled

Key Takeaway
An accusation against an outsider removes the collective qasamah obligation from the locality, based on the presumption of innocence for the community.

The Oath Wording in Qasamah When the Accused Names Another
Ruling on the wording of the oath when the accused points to another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exact wording of the oath in qasamah when the accused points to another, and explain the two elements of denial required.

Core Idea
When the accused names another killer, the oath must deny his own involvement and limit the accusation to that named person.

The qasamah procedure is designed to clear the accused from suspicion while preserving accountability for the victim. When the accused points to a specific person, the oath must be worded to exclude himself and restrict the accusation. This lesson dissects the required wording and its rationale as established in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The mustahlif (the person from whom the oath is taken) says 'So-and-so killed him' (قتله فلان). This triggers a specific oath formulation.
The mustahlif is the accused from the locality, not a witness.
2
Recognize the two required denials
The oath must contain two denials: (1) that the mustahlif himself did not kill the victim, and (2) that he knows no killer other than the named person.
The wording is: 'ما قتلت ولا علمت له قاتلا غير فلان'.
3
Understand the purpose
The purpose of qasamah is to exonerate the mustahlif from any involvement and to ensure that the accusation is confined to the named individual, not extended to others.
This protects both the mustahlif and unnamed parties from false implication.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, the judge must ensure the oath is recited exactly with both negations. Any deviation renders the oath incomplete.
The ruling is wajib; the specific wording is mandatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا قال المستحلف قتله فلان استحلف بالله ماقتلت ولا علمت له قاتلا غير فلان (۲٤۲۹) (۲۳) جس سے قسم لی جارہی ہے وہ کہے کہ فلاں نے قتل کیا ہے تو اس سے اس طرح قسم لی جائے گی کہ نہ میں نے قتل کیا ہے اور نہ کسی قاتل کو جانتا ہوں سوائے فلاں کے۔ جس آدمی سے قسم لی جارہی ہے وہ کہ رہا ہے کہ میرا اندازہ ہے کہ فلاں آدمی نے قتل کیا ہے تو قسم لینے میں دو باتوں کی رعایت کی جائے گی ۔ ایک تو یہ کہ میں نے قتل نہیں کیا ہے۔ اور دوسری یہ کہ فلاں آدمی کے علاوہ کسی اور کونہیں جانتا ہوں کہ اس نے قتل کیا ہوگا۔ قسامت کا مقصد یہ ہے کہ اپنی نفی ہو جائے اور مدعی علیہ کے علاوہ دوسروں کی بھی نفی ہو جائے۔
And if the one being sworn says 'So-and-so killed him', he is sworn by Allah that 'I did not kill him and I do not know any killer for him other than so-and-so.' (23) From whom the oath is being taken, if he says 'So-and-so killed him', then the oath will be taken from him in such a way that 'I did not kill him nor do I know any killer except so-and-so.' The person from whom the oath is taken is saying that it is his estimation that so-and-so killed him. So in taking the oath, two things are observed: one is that he did not kill, and the second is that he does not know anyone else besides so-and-so who might have killed him. The purpose of qasamah is that he negates himself and also negates others besides the accused.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage explains the exact wording of the oath in qasamah when the person being sworn (the accused from the locality) says that a specific other person (فلان) is the killer. The Hanafi procedure: the oath must include two denials: first, that he himself did not kill the victim; second, that he knows of no other killer besides the person he named. The Urdu commentary clarifies the rationale: the person is expressing his suspicion; to avoid admitting guilt, the oath must clear him of personal involvement and also limit the accusation to the named individual, thereby not implicating others. The purpose of qasamah is to exonerate oneself and avoid false implication of others.

Key Terms
مستحلف (mustahlif): The person from whom the oath is being taken, i.e., the accused from the locality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a person being sworn in qasamah says 'So-and-so killed him', what does he swear?
A) I swear that so-and-so killed him
B) I did not kill him and I know no killer other than so-and-so
C) I know nothing about the murder
D) So-and-so is lying

Key Takeaway
The qasamah oath when accusing a specific person must contain two denials: personal innocence and limited knowledge of the killer.

Testimony of Locals Against an Outsider is Rejected
Ruling on testimony admissibility in qasamah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the admissibility of testimony from locality members against an outsider in qasamah, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Testimony of two locals against an outsider is rejected because they are defendants, not witnesses.

This lesson examines a specific rule of evidence in cases of qasamah (oath-compurgation). When a murder occurs in a locality, the Hanafi school holds that inhabitants of that locality cannot testify against an outsider, as they themselves are under suspicion. We'll explore the rationale and an intra-Hanafi difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The text states: if two from the locality testify against an outsider that he killed the victim, their testimony is not accepted. Note the condition: witnesses must be from the locality where the murder occurred.
2
Understand the rationale (tuhmah)
The locals are themselves potential defendants in qasamah, so they have a personal interest in shifting blame. This creates suspicion (tuhmah) that makes their testimony unreliable.
The commentary explains they want to save their own necks.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that because the locals are not specifically designated as defendants (muta`ayyin), their testimony should be accepted. This differs from the majority position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text presents the majority view as the main ruling, and Abu Yusuf's view as an exception. In practice, the majority view (rejection) is relied upon.
Authority: Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا شهد اثنان من اهل المحلة على رجل من غيرهم انه قتله لم تقبل شهادتهما (۲٤۳۰) (۲۴) اگر محلہ والوں میں سے دو آدمی گواہی دے محلہ کے علاوہ کے آدمی پر کہ اس نے قتل کیا ہے تو ان دونوں کی گواہی قبول نہیں کی جائے گی۔ تو جس محلہ میں قتل ہوا ہے اس کے دو آدمی گواہی دے رہے ہیں کہ فلاں محلہ کے فلاں آدمی نے اس کو قتل کیا ہے تو ان دونوں آدمیوں کی گواہی مقبول نہیں ہے۔ اس محلے میں قتل ہونے کی وجہ سے یہ دونوں گواہ مدعی علیہ ہو گئے ۔ گویا کہ اپنی جان چھڑانے کے لئے گواہی دے کر دوسرے محلے والوں کی گردن پر ڈالنا چاہتے ہیں۔ اس لئے یہ متہم ہو گئے ۔ اس لئے ان کی گواہی مقبول نہیں ہوگی (۲) محلہ والے مدعی علیہ ہیں اس لئے ان پر قسم ہیں اس پر گواہی نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے بھی ان کی گواہی مقبول نہیں ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ متعین طور پر مدعی علیہ نہیں ہے اس لئے گواہی مقبول ہوگی۔
And if two from the people of the locality testify against a man from outside them that he killed him, their testimony is not accepted. (24) If two men from the locality give testimony against a person outside the locality that he killed him, then their testimony will not be accepted. So, in the locality where the murder occurred, two men are testifying that a man of that other locality killed him, so the testimony of these two men is not accepted. Because the murder happened in this locality, these two witnesses have become defendants (litigants). It is as if they want to save their own necks by giving testimony and putting it on the necks of the other locality. Therefore they become suspects. So their testimony is not accepted. (2) The people of the locality are defendants, so upon them are oaths, not testimony. For this reason also their testimony is not accepted. Imam Abu Yusuf says that they are not specifically defendants, so testimony would be accepted.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific rule of evidence in qasamah. When two witnesses from the locality where the body is found testify against an outsider, their testimony is rejected. The reason given is twofold: first, they are themselves potential defendants because the locality is collectively accused; therefore, they have a personal interest in shifting blame, making them unreliable (tuhmah). Second, the locality's role is to take oaths, not to give testimony, so they cannot act as witnesses. The Urdu commentary further explains that they are trying to save themselves by accusing someone else. Then, a note mentions Imam Abu Yusuf's opinion: he holds that the locals are not specifically designated as defendants (muda`a `alayhim) in a definitive sense, so he would accept their testimony. This indicates an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: the text's main ruling follows Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, rejecting the testimony, while Abu Yusuf accepts it. The commentary does not indicate which is mufta bihi, but usually the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is relied upon.

Key Terms
متعين (muta`ayyin): Specifically designated or identified as the defendant in a case.
تهمة (tuhmah): Suspicion of partiality or self-interest that disqualifies a witness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, what happens when two people from the locality where a murder occurred testify against an outsider?
A) Their testimony is accepted and the outsider is liable
B) Their testimony is rejected because they are defendants themselves
C) Only one of them can testify
D) The outsider must take an oath instead

Key Takeaway
In qasamah, the locality's members are not witnesses; their role is to take oaths, not to testify against outsiders.

Three Rulings on Qasamah: Dropping, Oath, and Witness Testimony
Expansion of a matn on three qasamah rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi rulings on qasamah and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding acceptance of testimony from locality residents against an outsider.

Core Idea
Qasamah drops when an outsider is accused; oath includes denial and knowledge; witnesses themselves cannot testify.

The text from Sharh al-Thamiri presents three key rulings from the Hanafi school on qasamah (collective oaths of compurgation). It explains when qasamah drops, how the oath is formulated, and whether residents of the locality can testify against an outsider. A significant intra-Hanafi disagreement arises on the third point between Imam Abu Yusuf and the majority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
List each ruling separately: (1) dropping of qasamah based on accusation, (2) content of the oath when the swearer names an alleged killer, (3) rejection of testimony from two residents against an outsider.
Each ruling has its own legal logic and consequence.
2
Understand the rationale for each
For the first, qasamah drops if an outsider is accused because the locality is no longer implicated. For the second, the oath covers both self-denial and denial of knowledge of any other killer. For the third, residents are considered defendants and their testimony to shift blame is unreliable.
The rationale ties back to the purpose of qasamah: exculpation of the locality and just accusation.
3
Analyze the intra-madhhab dispute
Imam Abu Yusuf accepts the testimony of two residents against an outsider if they are not individually accused, arguing they are not defendants. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) reject it because they are collectively defendants.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position rejects the testimony, following the majority view. The commentary implies the majority is stronger.
Knowing the mufta bihi is crucial for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القسامة
الولى على واحد من اهل المحلة بعينه لم تسقط القسامة عنهم [۲۴۲۸ (۲۲) وان ادعى على واحد من غيرهم سقطت عنهم [۲۴۲۹] (۲۳) واذا قال المستحلف قتله فلان استحلف بالله ماقتلت ولا علمت له قاتلا غير فلان [۲۴۳۰] (۲۴) واذا شهد اثنان من اهل المحلة على رجل من غيرهم انه قتله لم تقبل شهادتهما.
[۲۴۲۸] (۲۲) اور اگر محلے کے علاوہ میں سے کسی پر دعوی ہو تو محلے والے سے ساقط ہو جائے گی۔ جب محلے کے علاوہ آدمی پر قتل کا دعوی ہوا تو معلوم ہوا کہ محلے والے اس میں ملوث نہیں ہیں۔ اس لئے محلے والوں سے قسامت ساقط ہو جائے گی۔
[۲۴۲۹] (۲۳) جس سے قسم لی جارہی ہے وہ کہے کہ فلاں نے قتل کیا ہے تو اس سے اس طرح قسم لی جائے گی کہ نہ میں نے قتل کیا ہے اور نہ کسی قاتل کو جانتا ہوں سوائے فلاں کے۔ جس آدمی سے قسم لی جارہی ہے وہ کہ رہا ہے کہ میرا اندازہ ہے کہ فلاں آدمی نے قتل کیا ہے تو قسم لینے میں دو باتوں کی رعایت کی جائے گی ۔ ایک تو یہ کہ میں نے قتل نہیں کیا ہے۔ اور دوسری یہ کہ فلاں آدمی کے علاوہ کسی اور کونہیں جانتا ہوں کہ اس نے قتل کیا ہوگا۔ قسامت کا مقصد یہ ہے کہ اپنی نفی ہو جائے اور مدعی علیہ کے علاوہ دوسروں کی بھی نفی ہو جائے۔ ۲۴۳۰) (۲۴) اگر محلہ والوں میں سے دو آدمی گواہی دے محلہ کے علاوہ کے آدمی پر کہ اس نے قتل کیا ہے تو ان دونوں کی گواہی قبول نہیں کی جائے گی۔ تو جس محلہ میں قتل ہوا ہے اس کے دو آدمی گواہی دے رہے ہیں کہ فلاں محلہ کے فلاں آدمی نے اس کو قتل کیا ہے تو ان دونوں آدمیوں کی گواہی مقبول نہیں ہے۔ اس محلے میں قتل ہونے کی وجہ سے یہ دونوں گواہ مدعی علیہ ہو گئے ۔ گویا کہ اپنی جان چھڑانے کے لئے گواہی دے کر دوسرے محلے والوں کی گردن پر ڈالنا چاہتے ہیں۔ اس لئے یہ متہم ہو گئے ۔ اس لئے ان کی گواہی مقبول نہیں ہوگی (۲) محلہ والے مدعی علیہ ہیں اس لئے ان پر قسم ہیں اس پر گواہی نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے بھی ان کی گواہی مقبول نہیں ہے۔
امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ متعین طور پر مدعی علیہ نہیں ہے اس لئے گواہی مقبول ہوگی۔
Chapter of Qasamah. If the claimant accuses a specific person from the people of the locality, qasamah does not drop from them. And if he accuses someone outside them, qasamah drops from them. And if the one being sworn says, 'So-and-so killed him,' he is sworn by Allah: 'I did not kill, nor do I know any killer for him except so-and-so.' And if two witnesses from the people of the locality testify against a man from outside them that he killed him, their testimony is not accepted. [Commentary in Urdu explains each ruling, including the opinion of Imam Abu Yusuf that if the accused is not specifically identified, the testimony is accepted.]
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Thamiri presents three rulings on qasamah (collective oaths of compurgation) from the Hanafi madhhab, with a notable disagreement from Imam Abu Yusuf on the third issue. The text is a sharh (expansion) of a matn, and the author explains each ruling with reasoning.

First ruling (22): If the claimant accuses a specific person from the people of the locality (mahalla), the qasamah does not drop from the locality; they must still take the oath because the accusation is against someone among them. But if he accuses an outsider, qasamah drops from the locality because they are no longer implicated.

Second ruling (23): When the one being sworn says, 'So-and-so killed him,' the oath is formulated to include two negations: the swearer must swear that he did not kill nor does he know of any killer besides that specific person. This is because qasamah aims to exculpate the swearer and also to affirm the accusation against a third party. The swearer's statement is not a direct eye-witness but an opinion, so the oath covers both self-exculpation and the limited accusation.

Third ruling (24): If two witnesses from the locality testify against an outsider that he committed the murder, their testimony is not accepted. Reason: Since the murder occurred in their locality, the witnesses themselves are potential defendants in qasamah. They are testifying to shift blame, which makes them suspect. Additionally, the people of the locality are primarily defendants (required to take oaths) and cannot act as witnesses. However, Imam Abu Yusuf holds that if they are not specifically accused as individuals, their testimony is accepted because they are not actual defendants in that sense. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position according to the text is the majority view that their testimony is rejected.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasamah): A collective oath taken by the people of a locality to deny involvement in a murder when there is evidence of killing but no known killer.
محلة (mahalla): The locality or quarter where the murder occurred; the residents are collectively responsible to take the oath.
مستحلف (mustahlif): The person being sworn in the qasamah procedure; he takes the oath denying killing and knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two witnesses from the locality where a murder occurred testify against a man from another locality, what is the Hanafi ruling on their testimony according to the relied-upon position?
A) Their testimony is accepted because they are eyewitnesses.
B) Their testimony is rejected because they are considered potential defendants.
C) Their testimony is accepted only if they are not specifically accused.
D) Their testimony is rejected only if the defendant objects.

Key Takeaway
In qasamah, residents of the locality where the murder occurred are considered defendants and cannot testify against outsiders; their testimony is rejected to prevent self-exculpation.

Diyah and Aqila: Responsibility for Accidental and Quasi-Intentional Killing
Defines diyah and identifies the aqila as responsible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two categories of killing for which the aqila is liable and identify who constitutes the aqila (asaba or diwan).

Core Idea
Diyah for accidental and quasi-intentional killing is borne by the aqila, not the killer.

In Hanafi fiqh, the payment of blood money (diyah) for unintended killings is not solely the killer's burden. Instead, the aqila—a group of supporters—steps in to pay. This lesson explores the two types of killing that trigger aqila liability, who the aqila are, and the prophetic proof that establishes this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the killing types
The text specifies shibh al-amd (quasi-intentional) and khata' (accidental) as the categories where aqila pays. Intentional murder (amd) is excluded and requires qisas.
Shibh al-amd occurs when the killer intends the act but not death (e.g., striking with a stone).
2
Identify the aqila
The aqila are the people of the diwan (military register) if the killer is a soldier; otherwise, the asaba (agnatic relatives) like brothers, uncles, etc.
The commentary explains that if asaba cannot pay, the circle expands to more distant relatives.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith of Abu Huraira (Bukhari and Muslim) shows the Prophet ruling that diyah (aql) is on the agnates (asaba). This is the basis for aqila liability.
The key phrase is 'al-aqlu ala asabatiha'.
4
Apply the proof to the ruling
The hadith establishes that in accidental and quasi-intentional killings, the diyah is on the asaba. For soldiers, the diwan replaces the asaba due to custom and organized support.
5
Note cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school restricts aqila to asaba only, while Hanafi includes diwan. The Hanafi view is based on the prevailing practice of the early Muslim army.
This difference has practical implications for modern military and organised groups.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

خاندان والے اور عصبہ جودیت ادا کرتے ہیں ان کو عاقلہ کہتے ہیں۔

Book of al-Mawaqil (Diyah). [2431] (1) The diyah in shibh al-amd (quasi-intentional) and khata' (accidental), and every diyah that becomes obligatory by the very act of killing, is upon the aqila. [2432] (2) The aqila are the people of the diwan (military register) if the killer is from the people of the diwan. [Urdu commentary explains the derivation of 'aqila, the types of killing covered, and provides hadith proof from Abu Huraira about the aqila bearing the diyah.]
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Thamiri introduces the chapter on al-Mawaqil (the law of diyah payments by the aqila). The aqila are the responsible party for paying diyah (blood money) in cases of shibh al-amd (quasi-intentional killing) and khata' (accidental killing). The text provides two main rulings:

First ruling: Diyah for shibh al-amd and khata' is on the aqila. This excludes intentional murder (al-amd), where the killer alone is liable for qisas, and if he settles with a monetary payment, the aqila does not pay. The commentary clarifies that diyah for injuries (like cutting a limb) is also not on the aqila, whether intentional or accidental. The proof comes from the hadith of Abu Huraira: the Prophet (peace be upon him) ruled that diyah for a fetus (ghurra) and then when the pregnant woman died, he ruled that her inheritance goes to her children and husband, and 'the aqila is upon her agnates (asaba).' This hadith shows that in accidental and quasi-intentional killings, the aqila bear the responsibility. The Urdu commentary explains that shibh al-amd occurs when the killer intentionally strikes with a non-edge weapon (like a stone), causing death.

Second ruling: The aqila are the people of the diwan (military register) if the killer is among them. In the early Islamic army, soldiers were registered and grouped, and they served as aqila for each other. For non-soldiers, the aqila are the asaba (agnatic relatives) such as brothers, paternal uncles, etc. The commentary notes that if the asaba cannot pay, the responsibility spreads to more distant relatives.

Key Terms
عاقلة (aqila): The group responsible for paying diyah in cases of accidental or quasi-intentional killing; includes the asaba (agnatic relatives) or, for soldiers, the people of the diwan.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid as compensation for killing or injury; for killing, it is 100 camels or equivalent.
شبه عمد (shibh al-amd): Quasi-intentional killing: the killer intended an act but not death, e.g., striking with a stone or stick.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, on whom does the diyah for quasi-intentional killing (shibh al-amd) fall?
A) The killer alone
B) The aqila (agnates or diwan)
C) The victim's family
D) The state

Key Takeaway
Diyah for accidental and quasi-intentional killing is upon the aqila (agnates or diwan), not the killer.

The 'Aqila of Non-Registered Persons Is Their Tribe
Ruling that the 'aqila of a non-registered person is his tribe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the 'aqila for a person based on their registration status and apply the Hanafi rule using the hadith proof.

Core Idea
For those not in the state register, blood money is upon their tribe, not the state.

In Hanafi law, the 'aqila (those who pay diyah) differs based on a person's affiliation. For soldiers and stipend recipients registered in the government diwan, the 'aqila is the people of the register (i.e., the state system). For others, the obligation falls on their tribe (qabila), which comprises their male agnates (asaba). This lesson explores the ruling and its proof from the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of persons
The passage divides people into those in the government register (diwan) and those not. Understand that registration affects the composition of the 'aqila.
Registration typically applied to soldiers and stipend recipients.
2
State the ruling for each category
For the registered, the 'aqila is the people of the register; for the unregistered, it is the tribe. Commit this distinction.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith: 'The blood money is on the asaba' (Bukhari and Muslim). Identify that this is a hadith ahad with zanni thubut and dalala.
The hadith establishes the general principle that agnates bear the blood money.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'عقل' (al-'aql) means blood money, and 'عصبتها' (asabataha) means her paternal agnates. Thus the tribe is held responsible because they are the asaba.
The hadith is general, but the school distinguishes based on the condition of registration.
5
Note the cross-madhhab status
This ruling is mukhtalaf fih. The Shafi'i school also uses the asaba but does not make the same distinction regarding registration, leading to practical differences.
The thamarah al-khilaf: for a registered soldier, Hanafi assigns the diwan, while Shafi'i assigns the tribe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۴۳۴] ((۴) ومن لم يكن من اهل الديوان فعاقلته قبيلته [۲۴۳۴] (۴) جو لوگ دفتر والے نہ ہوں ان کا عاقلہ خاندان والے ہیں۔ اوپر گزر چکا ہے کہ جس کا نام دفتر میں ہے اس کا عاقلہ دفتر والے ہیں۔ اور جو لوگ دفتر والے نہیں ہیں ان کا عاقلہ خاندان والے ہیں۔ دلیل ، حدیث وغیرہ گزر چکی ہے۔ وان العقل على عصبتها ( بخاری شریف نمبر ۶۹۰۹ مسلم شریف ، نمبر ۱۶۸)
And whoever is not from the people of the register, his 'aqila is his tribe.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn clarifying who constitutes the 'aqila for different categories of people. The commentary explains that for those registered in the government register (diwan), the 'aqila is the people of the register; for those not registered, the 'aqila is their tribe. The proof is from hadith: 'The blood money is on the asaba (agnates)' (Bukhari and Muslim). The distinction likely arises because the registered have their stipends paid by the state, so their 'aqila is the state system; the unregistered rely on their clan.

Key Terms
ديوان (dīwān): The government register where names of soldiers or stipend recipients are recorded.
قبيلة (qabīla): Tribe or extended clan, the 'aqila for those not in the register.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is the 'aqila for a person not registered in the diwan?
A) The people of the register.
B) His tribe.
C) The state treasury.
D) The killer himself.

Key Takeaway
For those not registered in the state diwan, the 'aqila is the tribe, based on the hadith that blood money is on the asaba.

Distribution of Diyah Over Three Years
Ruling on installment method for diyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the installment method of diyah payment and calculate the annual per-person contribution for the 'aqila.

Core Idea
Diyah is paid in installments over three years, with each 'aqila member contributing no more than 4 dirhams total.

When a person is killed unintentionally, the blood money (diyah) of 10,000 dirhams (or equivalent) is due. This lesson explains how that payment is distributed fairly among the 'aqila (the offender's tribe or supporters) over a three-year period, ensuring no individual is overburdened.

Guided Path
1
Identify the total diyah and the payment period
The total diyah is set at 10,000 dirhams (or equivalent). The ruling states it is distributed (tuqassat) over three years, meaning the 'aqila pay in installments.
2
Determine the maximum individual contribution
No member of the 'aqila is required to pay more than 4 dirhams in total over the three years. This is the cap.
The cap prevents hardship on any individual.
3
Calculate the annual per-person amount
Since the cap is 4 dirhams total over 3 years, the annual amount is 4 ÷ 3 = 1 1/3 dirhams. In Hanafi coinage, 1 dirham = 6 daniq, so 1/3 dirham = 2 daniq. Thus, each person pays 1 dirham and 2 daniq per year.
This ensures equal distribution over time.
4
Understand the practical logic
With an annual per-person contribution of 1 1/3 dirhams, over three years each person pays exactly 4 dirhams. To collect the full 10,000 dirhams, the 'aqila must consist of at least 10,000 ÷ 4 = 2,500 members.
The number of 'aqila members ensures the total can be collected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۴۳۵](۵) تقسط عليهم في ثلث سنين لايزاد الواحد على اربعة دراهم في كل سنة درهم و دانقان وينقص منها. [۲۴۳۵] (۵) ان لوگوں پر قسط وار کر دی جائے گی تین سالوں میں۔ ایک آدمی پر چار درہم سے زیادہ نہ کیا جائے۔ ہر سال میں ایک درہم اور دو دانق اور چار سے کم بھی ہو سکتے ہیں۔ عاقلہ کے ہر آدمی سے چار درہم لیا جائے۔ اور چونکہ تین سال میں لینا ہے اس لئے ایک سال میں ایک درہم اور ایک تہائی یعنی دو دائق لیا جائے گا۔ اس اعتبار سے ۲۵۰۰ دو ہزار پانچ سو آدمیوں سے دیت لینی ہوگی تب دس ہزار درہم مکمل ہوں گے۔
It is distributed over them in three years; one person is not increased beyond four dirhams, in each year one dirham and two daniq, and they are reduced from it.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This ruling specifies the logistics of collecting diyah from the 'aqila. The total diyah of 10,000 dirhams (or equivalent) is distributed over three years. Each individual of the 'aqila pays a maximum of 4 dirhams total over the three years, which breaks down to 1 dirham and 2 daniq (i.e., 1 1/3 dirhams) per year. The commentary calculates that with 2500 people, the diyah is fully paid. This ensures that no one is burdened beyond a reasonable amount.

Key Terms
دانق (dāniq): A small coin or weight; 6 daniq = 1 dirham.
تقسط (tuqassat): Distributed in installments over time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How much does each 'aqila member pay per year if the diyah is fully covered by 2500 members?
A) 4 dirhams
B) 1 dirham and 2 daniq
C) 1 dirham
D) 2 dirhams

Key Takeaway
Diyah from the 'aqila must be paid over three years with each individual contributing no more than 4 dirhams total, ensuring affordability and fairness.

Adding the Nearest Tribe When 'Aqila Is Insufficient
Ruling on adding the nearest tribe if the primary tribe cannot afford the diyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when the 'aqila cannot afford the diyah and identify the criteria for adding other tribes.

Core Idea
If the tribe lacks enough members, the nearest tribe in lineage is added to share the diyah.

The 'aqila system distributes blood money among male relatives. But what if the tribe is too small to cover the full amount? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling on supplementing the 'aqila with the nearest other tribe, ensuring the diyah can be paid without exceeding the per-person cap of 4 dirhams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger condition
The ruling applies when the primary tribe does not have enough members (less than 2500 men) to cover the full diyah within the per-person limit.
The exact number 2500 comes from the maximum total raised if each of 2500 men contributes 4 dirhams = 10,000 dirhams, which is a typical diyah amount.
2
Determine which tribe is added
The next tribe in proximity of lineage is added. 'Nearest' means closest in kinship relation, not geography or alliance.
Proximity is measured by how close the ancestors are; e.g., paternal cousins' tribe before distant relatives.
3
State the ruling
It becomes obligatory (wajib) to include the nearest tribe to complete the number required for the 'aqila.
This ensures the diyah is paid without placing an excessive burden on any single individual beyond the specified limit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۴۳۶] (۶) فان لم تتسع القبيلة لذلك ضُمَّ اليهم اقرب القبائل من غيرهم [۲۴۳۶] (۶) اگر قبیلہ میں گنجائش نہ ہو تو ان کے ساتھ ملالئے جائیں گے قریبی قبیلے دوسرے کے۔ ایک قبیلے سے ۲۵۰۰ آدمی پورے نہ ہوتے ہوں تو رشتہ داری میں اس قبیلے سے جو زیادہ قریب ہو اس قبیلے کو دیت میں شامل کیا جائے گا تا کہ جتنا زیادہ لوگ ہوں اتنے ہی آسانی سے دیت ادا ہو سکے۔ کیونکہ ہر آدمی سے چار چار درہم ہی لئے جاسکیں گے۔
If the tribe is not spacious enough for that, then the nearest tribes from others are added to them.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This ruling addresses a shortfall in the 'aqila. If the tribe does not have enough members (2500) to cover the diyah within the per-person limit, then the nearest tribe in kinship is added to share the burden. The commentary explains that this ensures the payment can be made without exceeding the cap of 4 dirhams per person.

Key Terms
قبيلة (qabīla): Tribe; extended kinship group.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is another tribe added to the 'aqila?
A) When the first tribe has less than 2500 men.
B) When the first tribe refuses to pay.
C) When the killer requests it.
D) Always.

Key Takeaway
The nearest tribe in lineage must be added to the 'aqila when the primary tribe lacks sufficient members to pay the diyah within the limit of 4 dirhams per person.

Inclusion of the Killer in the 'Aqila
Dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i on killer's diyah liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the killer's contribution to diyah and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
The killer pays diyah like any 'aqila member; the hadith does not exempt him.

This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Shafi'i regarding the killer's liability for blood money (diyah). While both schools agree that the 'aqila (agnatic group) bears the primary responsibility, they differ on whether the killer himself must contribute. The Hanafi position includes the killer as a member of the 'aqila, requiring him to pay his share, whereas Imam Shafi'i exempts him based on a hadith. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to handle textual evidence and to see how different usul principles lead to different rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
The text states that the killer enters with the 'aqila and pays like any member. Recognize that the killer is not exempt.
2
Understand the Hanafi reasoning
Two reasons: (a) the crime originates from the killer, so he should bear some responsibility; (b) he is a member of the clan, so he shares the collective obligation.
3
State the Shafi'i counter-position
Imam Shafi'i rules that the killer pays nothing. His evidence: a hadith stating blood money is on the 'asaba (agnates), implying the killer is excluded.
4
Analyze the textual evidence
Examine the hadith: 'Wa qada anna diyat al-mar'ati 'ala 'aqilatiha' (The diyah of a woman is on her 'aqila). Does it exclude the killer? Hanafi responds that the hadith does not explicitly exclude the killer; the killer is part of the 'asaba.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Hanafi, the killer pays a portion (e.g., 4 dirhams over 3 years); under Shafi'i, he is fully exempt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۴۳۷] (۷) ويدخل القاتل مع العاقلة فيكون فيما يؤدى كاحدهم [۲۴۳۷] (۷) عاقلہ کے ساتھ قاتل بھی داخل ہوگا ۔ پس وہ دیت ادا کرنے میں ایک عاقلہ کی طرح ہوگا۔ جس طرح عاقلہ دیت ادا کرے گا اور قاتل بھی عاقلہ کے ایک فرد کی طرح شمار کیا جائے گا۔ چنانچہ عاقلہ کا ہر فرد تین سال میں چار درہم ادا کرے گا تو قاتل بھی تین سال میں چار درہم ادا کرے گا۔ اصل جرم قاتل کا ہے اس لئے اس کو بھی دیت ادا کرنی چاہئے (۲) خاندان کی طرح وہ بھی کہنے کا ایک فرد ہے اس لئے جس طرح اور فرد پر دیت ہے اس فرد پر بھی دیت ہوگی۔ انده امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ خود قاتل پر کچھ دیت نہیں ہوگی ۔ وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ حدیث میں دیت عصبہ پر ہے۔ اس لئے قاتل اس سے بری ہو جائے گا۔ وقضى ان دية المرأة على عاقلتها ( بخاری شریف، باب جنين المرأة وان العقل على الوالد ص ۰۲۰ انمبر ۶۹۱۰ )
And the killer enters with the 'aqila, so he is like one of them in what is paid.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Shafi'i. The Hanafi ruling (matn) states that the killer himself is part of the 'aqila and contributes to the diyah like any other member. The commentary argues that since the crime originates from him, he should share in the payment; also, he is a member of the family. Imam Shafi'i disagrees, holding that the killer pays nothing, based on hadith that blood money is on the 'asaba (agnates), which excludes the killer. The Hanafi position is that the killer is included because the hadith does not explicitly exclude him, and he is part of the 'asaba. The reference to a hadith about a woman's diyah being on her 'aqila is used as evidence for the general principle.

Key Terms
قاتل (qātil): The person who caused the accidental killing (killer).
عصبة (asaba): Agnatic relatives; those who inherit and are liable for blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does the killer of accidental death pay anything for the diyah?
A) No, he is exempt.
B) Yes, he pays like any other member of the 'aqila.
C) He pays double.
D) Only if he is wealthy.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires the killer to contribute to diyah as a member of the 'aqila, opposing the Shafi'i view of full exemption.

The 'Aqila of a Freed Slave
Ruling on the 'aqila of a freed slave with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the 'aqila of a freed slave and identify the hadith proof for this ruling.

Core Idea
The master's clan becomes the 'aqila for a freed slave, based on the hadith: 'The client of a people is one of them.'

This passage explains who is responsible for paying blood money (diyah) when a freed slave commits an accidental killing. Since a freed slave has no biological clan, the Hanafi school assigns this duty to the master who freed him and the master's tribe. The ruling is derived from a hadith that equates the client's status with membership in the clan. We will examine the proof and the broader concept of wala' (clientage).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the hukm: The 'aqila (responsible clan) for a freed slave is his master's clan. The master and his tribe must pay the diyah for any accidental killing committed by the freed slave.
The freed slave has no biological clan, so the master's clan substitutes.
2
Understand the Context
Recognize the situation: The slave is freed (mu'taq). He has no biological relatives to serve as 'aqila. Hence, the walā' (clientage) of emancipation transfers responsibility to the manumitter and his clan.
Two types of wala' exist: wala' al-itaq (emancipation) and wala' al-muwalat (contractual alliance).
3
Locate the Dalil
Find the proof: The hadith narrated by Ibn Abi Rafi' from Abu Rafi', where the Prophet ﷺ said: 'The client of a people is one of them (mawlā al-qawmi min anfusihim).' This places the freed slave within the master's clan.
The hadith is cited in Abu Dawud and Bukhari.
4
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
Understand how the hadith proves the ruling: The phrase 'min anfusihim' (one of them) indicates that the client shares the same status as members of the clan, including liability for diyah. Thus, the master's clan becomes the 'aqila.
The proof is zanni al-thubut (single transmission) and zanni al-dalalah (interpretation), but it is accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Differentiate from Muwalat
Contrast with wala' al-muwalat: If a person enters a mutual guarantee contract with another tribe, that tribe becomes the 'aqila. The passage notes that the contracting tribe pays the diyah for the contract party.
This distinction shows the source of responsibility: emancipation versus contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۴۳۸] (۸) وعاقلة المعتق [۲۴۳۸] (۸) آزاد شدہ کا عاقلہ اس کے آقا کا قبیلہ ہے۔ اور مولا موالات کی طرف سے دے گا اس کو مولی اور اس کا قبیلہ۔ جو غلام آزاد ہو گیا اب اس کے خاندان کا کوئی نہیں ہے صرف آزاد کرنے والا آقا اور اس کا قبیلہ ہے تو اس آزاد شدہ غلام کا عاقلہ آقا اور آقا کا قبیلہ ہو گا۔ اور وہی لوگ قتل خطا کی دیت ادا کریں گے۔ حدیث میں ہے کہ آقا اور اس کا قبیلہ ہی آزاد شدہ غلام کا عاقلہ ہوا اور قبیلہ ہوا اور وہ آقا ہی کے قبیلے میں شمار ہوگا۔ عن ابن ابی رافع عن ابی رافع ان النبى الله الله بعث رجلا على الصدقة من بن مخزوم فقال لابي رافع اصحبني فانك تصيب منها قال حتى أتى النبي الله فاساله فاتاه فسأله فقال مولى القوم من انفسهم وانا لا تحل لنا الصدقة(الف)(ابوداؤد شریف، باب الصدقۃ علی بنی ہاشم ص ۲۴۰ نمبر ۶۵۰ ار بخاری شریف ، باب مولی القوم من السهم وابن الاخت منهم ص ۹۹۹ نمبر ۶۱ ۶۷ ) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ آزاد کردہ غلام آقا کے خاندان میں سے ہے۔ اس لئے آقا کے خاندان ہی اس کی دیت ادا کریں گے۔ وہ لوگ جو دوسری قوم سے عہد و پیمان کر لیتے ہیں کہ میں جنایت کروں تو تم اس کی دیت ادا کرنا اور تم جنایت کرو تو میں اس کی دیت ادا کروں گا اس کو مولی موالات کہتے ہیں۔ پس اگر اس نے قتل خطا کی تو اس کی دیت مولی موالات ادا کریں گے۔
And the 'aqila of the freed slave is his master's clan.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This ruling explains who is the 'aqila for a freed slave. Since a freed slave has no biological clan, his 'aqila is his former master and the master's tribe. The commentary elaborates on two types of wala': wala' al-itaq (clientage of emancipation) and wala' al-muwalat (clientage by contract). For a freed slave, the master and his tribe assume responsibility. The proof is from the hadith: 'The client of a people is one of them.' This hadith is used to show that the freed slave belongs to the master's clan. Similarly, for those who enter a mutual guarantee contract (muwalat), the contracting party's clan becomes the 'aqila.

Key Terms
معتق (mu'taq): A freed slave; a person who was freed from slavery.
مولی الموالات (mawlā al-muwālāt): A client by contract of mutual alliance for blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is responsible for paying diyah for a freed slave who commits accidental killing?
A) The freed slave himself.
B) The state.
C) The master who freed him and his clan.
D) No one.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the master's clan is the 'aqila for a freed slave, as established by the hadith 'the client of a people is one of them'.

Abu Yusuf's Ta'zir Limit: 75 vs 39 Stripes
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ta'zir maximum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi positions on the maximum ta'zir stripes and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Ta'zir maximum: Abu Yusuf says 75 (analogy from 80 hadd), but the school relies on 39.

Imam Abu Yusuf proposes a maximum of 75 stripes for ta'zir, analogizing from the hadd of 80 stripes for a free person. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is 39 stripes, from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
The passage presents three views: Abu Yusuf (75), Abu Hanifa & Muhammad (39), and a third view (10 stripes based on a hadith). Identify each.
The hadith limiting to 10 is from different schools.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's analogy
Abu Yusuf uses qiyas: the hadd for a free person is 80 stripes (e.g., qadhf), so ta'zir, being less severe, should be 5 less, i.e., 75.
80 is the hadd; ta'zir is discretionary, so reduction is meant to keep it below hadd.
3
Contrast with the majority Hanafi view
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad set the maximum at 39. Why? The text doesn't detail their evidence here, but historically they rely on different analogies or consider the hadith on 10 but extend it slightly.
The Zahir al-Riwayah supports 39.
4
Note the external view and its hadith
Some imams (likely Shafi'i or others) limit to 10 stripes based on the hadith from Abu Burda: the Prophet prohibited flogging above 10 except in hadd punishments.
This hadith is in Bukhari and Muslim.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Among the three Hanafi positions, the school relies on Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (39 stripes). Abu Yusuf's view is known but not taken for fatwa.
This shows how the school weighs different opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابو يوسف يبلغ بالتعزير خمسة وسبعين سوطا. ر وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ آزاد کی حد اسی کوڑے ہیں اس لئے اس سے پانچ کوڑے کم کر کے پچھتر کوڑے تک لگا سکتا ہے. یعنی ایک کوڑے سے لیکر پچھتر کوڑے تک مار سکتے ہیں. بعض ائمہ کی رائے ہے کہ تعزیر دس کوڑے سے زیادہ نہ ہو. ان کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے. عن ابی بردة انه سمع رسول الله يقول لا يجلد احد فوق عشرة اشواط الا في حد من حدود الله.
And Abu Yusuf said: Ta'zir can reach seventy-five stripes. He says: The hadd for a free person is eighty stripes, so five stripes are reduced to reach seventy-five. That is, one can strike from one to seventy-five stripes. Some imams hold that ta'zir should not exceed ten stripes. Their evidence is the hadith: from Abu Burda that he heard the Messenger of Allah say: 'None should be flogged more than ten stripes except in a hadd from the limits of Allah.'
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the maximum of ta'zir. Imam Abu Yusuf analogizes from the hadd for a free person (80 stripes for zina for non-muhsan? Actually, the Hanafi view for free non-muhsan is 100; here 80 perhaps refers to qadhf? But the text says 'hadd for free person is 80' — likely referring to qadhf or a specific context. Abu Yusuf reduces by 5 to 75 as maximum. Note the main Hanafi position (39) is from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The text also mentions a contrary view limiting to 10 stripes based on a hadith from Muslim and Bukhari. The commentary follows the text: states Abu Yusuf's view, then his reasoning, then the opposing view with its evidence.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): Fixed punishment, here specifically the hadd for a free person (apparently 80 lashes).
تعزیر (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses not covered by hadd or qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 10
B) 39
C) 75
D) 80

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative position (mufta bihi) for maximum ta'zir stripes is 39, not 75 as per Abu Yusuf.
