Combining Ta'zir Flogging with Imprisonment
Ruling on adding imprisonment to flogging in ta'zir punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on combining imprisonment with flogging in ta'zir and explain the legal analogy (qiyas) that supports it.

Core Idea
In ta'zir, the judge may combine flogging and imprisonment by analogy from the hadd of zina.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in ta'zir: whether imprisonment can be added to flogging. The Hanafi school permits this, drawing an analogy from the Prophet's ﷺ treatment of the non-muhsan adulterer, who received both flogging and exile. We will explore the textual basis and the logical extension that the judge may combine punishments for discretionary crimes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the matn: 'يضم الى الضرب في التعزير الحبس'. What exactly is being ruled? That imprisonment is added to flogging in ta'zir. Note that this is a permissive ruling (mubah) for the judge, not an obligation.
The ruling is discretionary; the judge may choose to combine or not.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is from the hadd of zina for a non-muhsan: the Prophet ﷺ prescribed flogging and exile. This hadith establishes the principle that corporal punishment can be combined with another form of punishment (exile) in a fixed hadd.
Hadith: 'أمر فيمن زنى ولم يحصن جلد مائة وتغريب عام' (Bukhari)
3
Trace the qiyas
Apply qiyas: the original case (asl) is the hadd of zina where flogging and exile are combined. The new case (far') is ta'zir where we consider combining flogging and imprisonment. The effective cause (illa) is the permissibility of combining a corporal punishment with a non-corporal measure to achieve deterrence. Since the ruling is based on a clear text (nass) and the cause is common, the ruling transfers to ta'zir.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) because the illa is evident.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., some earlier Hanbali or Shafi'i views) hold that ta'zir should be restricted to flogging alone. The Hanafi school responds by emphasizing the discretionary nature of ta'zir and the broader analogy from the hadd.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih (disputed).
5
Evaluate the discretion
The ruling grants the qadi (judge) flexibility. He may impose flogging alone, imprisonment alone, or both, depending on the circumstances. This is based on the principle that ta'zir aims at reform and deterrence.
Modern parallel: discretionary sentencing in many legal systems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يضم الى الضرب في التعزير الحبس فعل [۱۲۵۱۹ (۲۲) واشد الضرب التعزير ثم حد الزنا ثم حد الشرب ثم حد القذف [ ۲۵۲۰] (۲۳) ... انظر الصفحة التالية.
Imprisonment is added to flogging in ta'zir. (Then the text moves to severity of flogging, but the lesson on combining imprisonment comes from the commentary).
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling that in ta'zir, the judge may combine flogging with imprisonment if he deems it appropriate. The proof is taken from the sunnah in the case of zina for the non-muhsan: the Prophet ﷺ prescribed flogging and exile for one year (as per the hadith of Zaid ibn Khalid al-Juhani). By analogy, in ta'zir, the judge may also combine flogging with imprisonment. The text emphasizes that this is discretionary; the judge decides based on circumstances.

Key Terms
حبس (habs): Imprisonment, discretionary detention as part of ta'zir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Direct Qur'anic verse
B) Analogy from the hadd of zina for non-muhsan
C) Analogy from the hadd of qadhf
D) Consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a ta'zir punishment may combine flogging with imprisonment at the judge's discretion, based on analogy from the hadd of zina.

Exemptions from Hand Amputation in Theft
List of theft exemptions with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five categories of items exempt from hadd amputation under Hanafi law and cite the specific evidence (hadith/athar) for each exemption.

Core Idea
The hand is cut only for valuable, protected, owned property: trivial, perishable, unprotected, or forbidden items are exempt.

The hadd penalty for theft (amputation of the hand) is one of the most severe punishments in Islamic law, but it is not applied to every theft. The Hanafi school carefully limits it to theft of valuable, protected property. This passage from al-Sharh al-Thamiri details the categories of items that are exempt, explaining the reasoning and supporting each rule with prophetic hadith and companion athar. By understanding these exemptions, you will grasp the precision and mercy in Hanafi criminal jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Theft of valuable protected property incurs the hadd amputation. But for certain categories, the hadd is not applied. Begin by recognising that the passage lists exceptions to the general rule.
The general rule: hand is cut for theft of a nisab (value of 10 dirhams) from a secured place.
2
List the exempt categories
Read the passage carefully and extract the five categories explicitly mentioned: (1) trivial items (tafih), (2) mubah al-asl items (originally permissible), (3) unprotected items (ghayr mahfuz), (4) perishable items (like fresh fruits, milk), and (5) intoxicating drinks and musical instruments (because they are haram to own or for wasting).
Some categories overlap; the key is understanding the rationale.
3
Locate the textual proof for each category
The author cites specific hadith and athar: e.g., 'لا قطع في ثمر ولا كثر' for fruits and blossom; the story of the man who stole food; the hadith on intoxicating drinks and nardashir (backgammon). Note the chain and the wording of each proof.
Know the sources: Abu Dawud, Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, etc.
4
Analyse the illa (legal cause) behind the exemption
For perishable items, the illa is 'yusri'u ilayhi al-fasad' (quick to spoil). For unprotected items, it is being in a non-secured location. For intoxicants, the illa is that they are not considered valuable property because they are haram. Connect each exemption to its underlying reason.
The Hanafi school emphasises these illa to limit the hadd.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi and cross-madhhab difference
This is the established Hanafi position. Note that the Shafi'i school may cut for fruit once it is picked and stored in a secure place. Understand the practical consequence (thamarah) of this difference.
The passage shows that even high-value items like a falcon are cut if they are mubah al-asl.

[۲۵۲۸] (۶) اور کاٹنا نہیں ہے مستی اور شرابوں میں اور نہ باجے میں۔
جائے گا۔
پینے کی نشہ آور چیز چرالے تو اس میں ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا ، اسی طرح باجے کی چیز مثلا ڈھول تاشا چہالے تو اس میں ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا
یہ چیزیں برباد کرنے کی ہیں۔ ان کو بہا دینا چاہئے اور توڑ دینا چاہئے اس لئے ہو سکتا ہے کہ اس کا چہرانا بر باد کرنے اور بہانے کے لئے ہو۔ اس لئے نہیں کاٹا جائے گا (۲) حدیث میں کھیل کود کی چیزوں کے بارے میں سخت وعید ہے۔ عن سليمان بن بريدة عن ابيه ان النبي مال الله قال من لعب بالنردشير فكأنما صبغ يده في لحم خنزير و دمه (ب) (مسلم شریف، باب تحریم اللعب بالنردشیر ص ۲۴۰ نمبر ۲۲۶۰) کتاب الشعر ) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ نردشیر جو کھیل کود کی چیز ہے اس سے نہیں کھیلنا چاہئے بلکہ اس کو توڑ دینا چاہئے۔ اس لئے جب وہ قیمتی نہیں رہی تو اس کے چرانے میں ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اور نشہ آور چیزوں کے بارے میں یہ حدیث ہے۔ ان عائشة قالت ... فقال رسول الله الا الله كل شراب اسكر فهو حرام (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب الخمر من العسل هو البقع ص ۸۳۶ نمبر ۵۵۸۶) اور شراب بنانے والے برتنوں کے بارے میں فرمایا کہ ان میں نبیذ بھی نہ بناؤ. عن على قال نهى النبي الله عن الدباء والمزفت (و) ( بخاری شریف، باب ترخیص النبی ملی فی الاوعیہ والظروف بعد انھی ، ص ۱۸۳۷، نمبر ۵۵۹۴) جب ان برتوں میں نبیذ بنانے سے منع فرمایا تو نشہ آور چیزوں کے چرانے سے کیسے ہاتھ کٹے گا (۴) ایک اثر سے بھی استدلال کی جا سکتا ہے۔ عن ابن جريج سمعت بعض من ارضى يقول لا قطع في باز سرق وان كان ثمنه دينارا فاكثر من ذلك (ه) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۸۸ الرجل يسرق الطیر اوالبازی ما علیه؟ ج ثامن ص ۵۱۸ نمبر ۲۸۶۰۱) اس اثر میں ہے کہ باز کے چرانے سے ہاتھ نہیں کٹے گا اور باز شکار کے لئے
حاشیه : (الف) حضرت سفیان نے فرمایا جو چیزیں دن میں خراب ہو جاتی ہیں ان کو بقاء نہیں ہے جیسے ٹرید، گوشت وغیرہ تو اس میں ہاتھ کا نشانہیں ہے لیکن تعزیر کی جائے گی۔ اور پھل درخت پر ہو تو اس کے چرانے میں ہاتھ کا ٹا نہیں ہے لیکن تعزیر کی جائے گی (ب) آپ نے فرمایا کوئی نردشیر کھیلے تو گویا کہ اپنے ہاتھ کو سور کے گوشت اور اس کے خون میں رنگا (ج) آپ نے فرمایا ہر پینے کی چیز جس میں نشہ ہو وہ حرام ہے (د) آپ نے کدو اور تارکول سے رنگے ہوئے برتن سے منع فرمایا(1) ابن جریج کو کہتے سنادہ فرماتے ہیں کہ ایسے آدمی کو کہتے سنا ہوں جس سے میں راضی ہوں، باز چرالے تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا چاہے اس کی قیمت ایک دیناریا اس سے
زیادہ ہو۔
Islam is like wood, grass, reeds, fish, game, and birds [2527] (5) And not in what quickly perishes like fresh fruits, milk, meat, watermelon, fruit on trees, and crop that has not been harvested. From this hadith it is understood that if someone steals animal fodder from a mountain, his hand will not be cut. From the above athar it is also understood that in game (hunting) the hand is not cut because in the above athar it says 'no cutting in birds', meaning in hunting the hand is not cut. Because these things are mubah al-asl in dar al-Islam. The principle: the hand is cut for valuable items. If it is (1) a trivial item, (2) mubah al-asl, (3) unprotected, then his hand will not be cut for stealing them. Tafih: lowly item, khashab: wood, hashesh: grass, al-qasab: reed. [2527] (5) Also his hand will not be cut for what quickly perishes like fresh fruits, milk, meat, watermelon, fruits on the tree, and crop that has not been harvested. Things that quickly perish are not so precious and excellent that the hand, a great limb, should be cut for them. Like fresh fruits, milk, meat, watermelon etc., or what is on the tree or crop still in the field, they are not in a protected place. Therefore the hand will not be cut for stealing them. In the hadith, there are proofs for both. Rafi' said: I heard the Messenger of Allah say: 'No cutting in fruit and no cutting in [unripe] fruit.' (alif) Another narration: from Amr ibn al-As from the Messenger of Allah that he was asked about hanging dates and he said: 'Whoever takes some in his mouth out of need, not taking a pouchful, there is nothing upon him. Whoever takes something out with him, upon him is a double restitution and punishment. And whoever steals something from it after it has been taken to the threshing floor and it reaches the value of a shield, then cutting is upon him.' (be) From this hadith it is understood that the hand is not cut for stealing fruit and blossom because they quickly perish and are also unprotected and fresh. This hadith also shows that if the crop has not been cut, the hand will not be cut. (3) In an athar: from al-Hasan that the Prophet was brought a man who had stolen food and he did not cut his hand. (jeem) This athar shows that when food is stolen, the hand is not cut. Therefore, the hand is not cut for stealing meat and milk because they quickly perish. Sufyan said: And it is the one. Footnote: (alif) The Prophet says: There is no cutting for stealing fruit and blossom. (be) The Prophet was asked about hanging dates and he said: A needy person may eat from his mouth without filling his pouch, then there is nothing upon him, i.e., no compensation. And if someone takes fruit away, he has to pay double compensation and punishment. And if someone steals after it reaches the threshing floor and it reaches the price of a shield, then cutting is upon him. (jeem) A man was brought to the Prophet who had stolen food, and he did not cut his hand. [PAGE_418] Al-Sharh al-Thamiri Volume 3, 413, Kitab al-Sariqa. The crop that has not been harvested [2528] (6) And there is no cutting in intoxicating drinks nor in the tambourine. It spoils within the day, it has no durability. For tharid, meat, and the like, there is no cutting, but he is punished (ta'zir). And when the fruit is on its tree, then there is no cutting, but he is punished. (alif) From this athar it is understood that the hand is not cut for meat and similar perishable items. For something that quickly perishes, or is in an unprotected place, or is mubah al-asl, the hand will not be cut. Al-fawakih: fruits, al-laban: milk, al-battikh: watermelon, lam yuhsad: crop not harvested. [2528] (6) And there is no cutting for intoxicating drinks and not for musical instruments. If someone steals an intoxicating drink, his hand will not be cut. Similarly, if someone steals a musical instrument like a drum or tabla, his hand will not be cut. These things are for wasting. They should be spilled and broken. So it is possible that stealing them is for wasting and spilling. Therefore the hand will not be cut. (2) There is a severe warning in hadith regarding playthings. From Sulayman ibn Burayda from his father that the Prophet said: 'Whoever plays with nardashir, it is as if he dipped his hand in the flesh of a pig and its blood.' (be) (Muslim) From this hadith it is understood that nardashir, which is a plaything, should not be played with but should be broken. So when it is not valuable, the hand will not be cut for stealing it. Regarding intoxicating drinks, there is this hadith: Aisha said... the Messenger of Allah said: 'Every drink that intoxicates is haram.' (jeem) (Bukhari) And regarding vessels used for making wine, he forbade making nabidh in them. From Ali: The Prophet forbade the gourd and the tarred container. (dal) (Bukhari) When he forbade making nabidh in these vessels, then how can the hand be cut for stealing intoxicants? (4) It can be deduced from an athar: From Ibn Jurayj: I heard some reliable person say: There is no cutting for stealing a falcon, even if its price is a dinar or more. (he) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba) In this athar, the hand will not be cut for stealing a falcon, and the falcon is for hunting. Footnote: (alif) Sufyan said: Things that spoil during the day have no permanence, like tharid, meat etc., there is no cutting, but ta'zir is given. And if the fruit is on the tree, there is no cutting for stealing it, but ta'zir is given. (be) The Prophet said: Whoever plays nardashir, it is as if he dipped his hand in pig's flesh and its blood. (jeem) The Prophet said: Every intoxicating drink is haram. (dal) The Prophet forbade the gourd and the tarred vessel. (1) Ibn Jurayj heard a reliable person say: If a falcon is stolen, the hand will not be cut, even if its price is a dinar or more.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage from al-Sharh al-Thamiri discusses the conditions under which the hadd penalty of hand amputation is not applied in theft. The author lists several categories of items that are exempt: (1) items that quickly perish (like fresh fruits, milk, meat), (2) items that are originally permissible (mubah al-asl) in Dar al-Islam (like wood, grass, game birds), (3) items that are not in protected storage (like fruit on trees, unharvested crop), and (4) trivial or low-value items (like small amounts of food). The author also adds intoxicating drinks and musical instruments, as they are usually acquired for waste or are forbidden to own. Each category is supported by hadith or athar. For example, the hadith 'لا قطع في ثمر ولا كثر' exempts fruit and blossom, and the story of the man who stole food shows that food items do not incur amputation. For intoxicants, the hadith that every intoxicant is haram implies that such items are not considered protected property worthy of amputation. The passage concludes that the hand is not cut for these items, but ta'zir (discretionary punishment) may be applied. The Hanafi school follows these rulings as part of its detailed conditions for applying the hadd of theft.

Key Terms
مباح الاصل (mubāḥ al-aṣl): Items that are inherently permissible to own, such as wild animals, wood, grass; theft of such items does not incur hadd because they are not owned property.
تافه (tāfih): Trivial or low-value item, insufficient to warrant amputation.
القطع في الثمر ولا كثر (al-qaṭʿ fī al-thamar wa lā kathar): No cutting for fruit and blossom (unripe fruit) – a hadith exempting fresh produce from amputation.
تعزير (taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment administered by the judge for crimes that do not have a fixed hadd or as a substitute when hadd conditions are not met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a valid reason for not applying the hadd amputation in theft?
A) The item is a musical instrument.
B) The item is stolen from a locked warehouse.
C) The item is a valuable diamond.
D) The item is a wild deer caught from the forest.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school carefully limits the hadd amputation to theft of valuable, protected, owned property, exempting items that are trivial, originally permissible, unprotected, quickly perishable, or forbidden to own.

Exemptions from Hand Amputation: Master, Spouse, and Relative
Rulings and proofs for exemption from hand amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five categories of individuals exempt from hand amputation for theft according to the Hanafi school and explain the underlying principle using the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas.

Core Idea
Shared ownership or overlapping rights lift the hadd penalty for theft.

The hadd punishment for theft is severe, but the Hanafi school recognizes that certain relationships—master-slave, husband-wife, close relatives, and shared booty—create a form of common ownership. In such cases, the Prophet (SAW) said, 'The property of Allah; some of it stole from some,' indicating that the special relationship precludes amputation. This lesson examines the specific exemptions and their evidential basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exempt categories
From the text, list the five situations where hand amputation is not applied: slave stealing from master, master from mukatab, wife from husband, husband from wife, and close relative (mahram) from relative. Also includes theft from ghanimah (booty).
These are distinct from general theft conditions.
2
Locate the evidence for each
For slave-master, the athar of Ibn Mas'ud and 'Umar; for spouses, the athar of 'Amir and 'Abd al-Karim; for relatives, the statement of al-Thawri; for booty, the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas.
Note that the booty exemption also uses the Ibn 'Abbas hadith.
3
Extract the core principle
The hadith of Ibn 'Abbas provides a general principle: when property is shared (e.g., Allah's property), theft among the co-owners does not trigger hadd. The phrase 'some of it stole from some' indicates overlapping ownership.
This is a qiyas-based extension from the khums case to other shared-property situations.
4
Understand the legal reasoning
The exemption is based on the absence of complete, exclusive ownership in the victim. The thief has a rightful claim or the property is considered common. Thus, the strict hadd condition of private ownership is not fully met.
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to apply this principle to spouses and relatives, even though their ownership is not literally shared.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i generally requires amputation for theft from a master if the slave is not owned by that master, but agrees on the master's own property. The Hanafi position is more expansive in exempting master-slave and spouse theft.
The difference arises from varying interpretations of the hadith and the weight given to athar.

Or from the wife of his master, or from the husband of his mistress, or the master from his mukatab [2541] (19) And similarly between them. So if a slave steals from his master, or a master steals from his mukatab slave, or a wife steals from her husband, or a husband steals from his wife, the hand will not be cut. (2) There is an athar: Ibn Mas'ud was asked: 'My slave stole the cloak of my [another] slave.' He said: 'Your property; some of it stole from some. No cutting [of hand] on it.' That is the saying of Ibn Abbas (a). (Sunan Bayhaqi, Bab al-Abd yasriqu min mata' sayyidihi, vol.8 p.488 no.17302; Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, vol.5 p.514 no.28559) (2) There is an athar: 'Umar asked him: 'What did he steal?' He said: 'He stole the mirror of my wife, worth sixty dirhams.' He said: 'Send him back; there is no cutting on him; your servant stole your goods.' (b) (Sunan Bayhaqi, Bab al-Abd yasriqu min mal imra'ati sayyidihi, vol.8 p.489 no.17303) From these athar it is understood that if a slave steals from his master's property, his hand will not be cut. Regarding wife and husband, there is this athar: It reached me from 'Amir: 'There is no cutting on the husband of a woman regarding theft of her goods.' And in another narration: 'Abd al-Karim said: 'There is no cutting on the woman regarding theft of her husband's goods.' (c) (Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, Bab man saraqa malan la qat' fihi, vol.10 p.221 no.18908) And in the same chapter, hadith no.18907 is about a close relative (dhi rahim mahram). He said: 'Al-Thawri considered it good that one who steals from a close relative mahram, like his maternal uncle or paternal uncle or a close female relative, should not have his hand cut.' (d) (Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, vol.10 no.18907) From this athar it is understood that if a wife steals from her husband or a husband from his wife, or a close relative mahram steals, the hand will not be cut. The property of a mukatab is the property of his master, so if the master steals from his mukatab, his hand will not be cut. For all these rulings, the above hadith from Ibn Majah Sharif is evidence: From Ibn 'Abbas: 'A slave from the khums captives stole from the khums, and this was raised to the Prophet (SAW), and he did not cut his hand and said: 'The property of Allah; some of it stole from some.' (e) (Ibn Majah Sharif, Bab al-'abd yasriq, p.272 no.2590) At the end of this hadith: 'Some of it stole from some,' so the hand is not cut. (2) There is also this athar: From al-Thawri: 'If a mukatab steals something from his master, his hand is not cut; and if the master steals something from the mukatab, his hand is not cut.' (w) (Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-khiyanah, vol.10 p.211 no.1887). (2541) (19) Similarly, if one steals from war booty (ghanimah), his hand will not be cut. Commentary: (a) ... (b) ... (c) ... (d) ... (e) ... (w) ... The thief from booty [2542 (20) And hirz is of two types: a hirz due to its own meaning, like houses and rooms, and a hirz by the guard. [2543] (21) Whoever steals something from a hirz or from a non-hirz while its owner is with it... And a hadith has passed: 'A slave from the khums captives stole from the khums...' (Ibn Majah no.2590) Therefore, if one steals from ghanimah, the hand is not cut. (2) The thief also has a share in ghanimah, so it is also not cut.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on the chapter of theft, presents rulings on who is exempt from hand amputation due to a special relationship with the owner. The text establishes that a slave is not amputated for stealing from his master, nor a master from his slave (including a mukatab), nor spouses from each other, nor a close relative (mahram) from his relative. The reason given is that the property is effectively shared or common, as indicated by the phrase 'some of it stole from some.' The evidence includes athar (sayings of the Companions) such as Ibn Mas'ud and 'Umar, and a hadith from Ibn 'Abbas regarding a theft from the khums (one-fifth share of booty). The hadith: 'The property of Allah; some of it stole from some' is used as a principle. The ruling extends to war booty (ghanimah) because the thief himself has a claim to it. The passage then transitions to a discussion of hirz (protected place), but the first lesson focuses on these specific exemptions.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, paying for his freedom in installments. His property is considered his master's property.
ذِي رَحِمٍ مَحْرَمٍ (dhi rahim mahram): A relative who is within the prohibited degrees of marriage (e.g., uncle, aunt, sibling). Theft from such a relative does not incur hand amputation.
غنيمت (ghanimah): War booty. Theft from the common pool of booty does not incur hand amputation because the thief himself has a share in it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a wife steals from her husband, what is the ruling on hand amputation?
A) Her hand is cut because theft is theft.
B) Her hand is not cut because of the marital relationship.
C) Her hand is cut only if the value exceeds 10 dirhams.
D) Her hand is cut only if she has no children.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts theft from hand amputation when the property is effectively shared or the thief has a special relationship, based on the hadith 'some of it stole from some' and companion athar.

The Two Types of Hirz and Their Impact on the Hadd of Theft
Definition of hirz into two types with ruling and hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of hirz (by itself and by guard) and apply the condition of hirz to determine when hand amputation is wajib.

Core Idea
Theft from a protected place (hirz) is required for hand amputation; the protection can be intrinsic or by a guard.

The Hanafi school defines hirz as a condition for applying the hadd of theft. This passage from Al-Sharh al-Sameri explains that hirz can be intrinsic (like a locked house) or situational (when a guard protects the property). Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining when the severe penalty of hand amputation applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of hirz
The author states that hirz is of two types: one is a place that is inherently protective like a house or room; the other is a place made protective by the presence of a guard (muhafiz).
The first type does not require a person; the second requires a guard even in a non-protective place.
2
Understand the ruling for each type
If someone steals property worth ten dirhams or more from either type of hirz, hand amputation becomes wajib. The passage explicitly states this for both scenarios.
Nisab (minimum value) is also a condition.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
Two hadith are cited: one where a man's hand was cut for stealing a shield from a women's room (proving a room is a hirz by itself), and another where Safwan's cloak was stolen from under his head in the mosque (proving that personal guarding creates a hirz).
These are the proofs from Sunnah for the two types.
4
Apply to a scenario
Consider a mosque: without a guard, it is not a hirz; with the owner present and protecting his property, it becomes a hirz by guard. Stealing from the owner in that state incurs amputation.
This distinction is often misunderstood.

[۲۵۴۲] (۲۰) حرز دو طرح کے ہیں، ایک یہ کہ وہ جگہ حفاظت کی ہو جیسے گھر اور کمرہ، اور حرز محافظ کے ذریعہ۔ جس حرز سے چرانے سے ہاتھ کہتا ہے وہ دو طرح سے ہوتی ہیں۔ ایک تو یہ کہ وہ مکان ہی حفاظت کے لئے ہو ۔ جیسے گھر یا کمرہ ہے کہ اس میں آدمی نہ بھی ہو تو خود گھر اور کمرہ محافظ کے معنی میں ہے۔ ان کے اندر کوئی آدمی چرائے گا تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ اور دوسری صورت یہ ہے وہ کھلا ہوا میدان ہے یا مسجد ہے جس میں عام لوگ جاتے رہتے ہیں اس لئے وہ محافظ کے معنی میں نہیں ہے۔ لیکن وہاں آدمی حفاظت کے لئے بیٹھا ہوا ہے اس لئے آدمی کی حفاظت کی وجہ سے حرز بن گیا۔ اب محافظ کے پاس سے کوئی چرائے گا تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ گھر یا کمرہ خود محافظ اور حرز ہیں ان کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ ان عبد الله بن عمر حدثهم ان النبي له قطع يد رجل سرق ترسا من صفة النساء ثمنه ثلاثة دراهم (الف ) ( ابو داؤد شریف، باب ما يقطع فيه السارق ص ۲۵۴ نمبر ۴۳۸۶) اس حدیث میں صفتہ النساء عورتوں کے کمرہ سے معلوم ہوا کہ کمرہ خود محافظ اور حرز ہے۔ اور آدمی کے ذریعہ حرز اور حفاظت ہو اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ ع صفوان بن امية قال كنت نائما في المسجد على خميصة لى ثمن ثلاثين درهما فجاء رجل فاختلسها منى فاخذ

حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے ایک آدمی کا ہاتھ کاٹا جس نے عورت کے چبوترے سے ڈھال چرائی تھی جس کی قیمت تین درہم تھی (ب ) حضرت صفوان بن امیہ فرماتے ہیں کہ میں مسجد میں اپنی کالی چادر پر سویا ہوا تھا جس کی قیمت میں درہم تھی۔ پس ایک آدمی آیا اور اس کو مجھ سے اچک لیا۔ پس آدمی پکڑا گیا اور حضور کے پاس لایا گیا تو آپ نے اس کے ہاتھ کاٹنے کا حکم دیا۔
[2542] (20) Hirz is of two types: a hirz due to its own meaning, like houses and rooms, and a hirz by the guard. The hirz from which stealing requires hand cutting is of two types. One: that the place itself is protective, like a house or room; even if no one is inside, the house or room itself serves as a guard. If someone steals from inside them, his hand will be cut. The other type: an open field or a mosque where people generally go; it is not protective in itself. But if there is a person sitting there for protection, it becomes a hirz due to the person's protection. If someone steals from near that guard, his hand will be cut. The evidence for house or room being self-guarding is the hadith: 'Ibn 'Umar narrated that the Prophet (SAW) cut the hand of a man who stole a shield from a women's room (suffat al-nisa') worth three dirhams.' (a) (Abu Dawud Sharif, Bab ma yuqta' fihi al-sariq, p.254 no.4386) This hadith shows that a room is itself a guard and a hirz. The evidence for hirz by a person is the hadith: 'Safwan b. Umayya said: I was sleeping in the mosque on my black cloak worth thirty dirhams. A man came and snatched it from me. The man was caught and brought to the Prophet (SAW), so he ordered that his hand be cut.' (b) (Abu Dawud Sharif, Bab fiman saraqa min hirz, p.255 no.4394; Nasa'i Sharif, Ma yakun hirzan wa ma la yakun, p.673 no.4885) In this hadith, Safwan had the cloak under his head, so he himself was its guard; due to human protection, the thief's hand was cut. [2543] (21) If someone steals something from a hirz or from a non-hirz while its owner is guarding it, then cutting is obligatory. If he steals something from a guarded place (even if the owner is not present) worth ten dirhams, his hand is cut. And if it was not a guarded place but the owner was guarding it and someone stole, his hand is also cut. The above hadith of Safwan [illustrates]: he was sleeping in the mosque, which is not a hirz because it is a place where everyone comes and goes, but he himself was guarding the cloak by putting it under his head; thus due to human protection, the thief's hand was cut. Commentary footnotes: (a) The Prophet cut the hand of a man who stole a shield from a women's platform worth three dirhams. (b) Safwan b. Umayya said: I was sleeping in the mosque on my black cloak worth thirty dirhams; a man came and snatched it from me; the man was caught and brought to the Prophet, who ordered his hand to be cut.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the concept of 'hirz' (protected place) which is a crucial condition for applying hand amputation in theft. The author states that hirz is of two types: (1) by itself (i.e., inherently protective places like houses, rooms, shops) and (2) by a guard (a person who protects the property). The ruling is that stealing from either type of hirz incurs the hadd of hand amputation, provided the nisab (value of ten dirhams) is reached. The passage then adds that even if the place is not inherently a hirz (like a mosque or open field), if the owner himself is present and guarding the property, it becomes a hirz by human presence. The evidence are two hadith: one where a man's hand was cut for stealing a shield from a women's room (showing a room is intrinsically a hirz), and another where Safwan was sleeping in the mosque with his cloak under his head (showing that the owner's personal guarding makes a non-hirz place effectively a hirz). The lesson emphasizes that hand amputation is not merely for taking property but for taking it from a place of protection.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A place that provides protection for property, either intrinsically (like a locked house) or by a guard. Theft from a hirz is a condition for hand amputation.
محافظ (muhafiz): A guard (person) who protects property. His presence makes any place a hirz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a valid hirz?
A) A locked house.
B) A guarded shop.
C) A mosque with a sleeping owner guarding his property.
D) An open field with no guard.

Key Takeaway
Hirz is of two types: by itself (like a house) and by guard; theft from either type, with nisab, incurs hand amputation.

No Hadd for a Guest's Theft from Host
Ruling on theft by a guest from host

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft by a guest and explain why the host's house is not considered a hirz for the guest.

Core Idea
A guest who steals from the host is exempt from hadd because the host's house is not a hirz for him.

In this lesson, we examine a specific exception to the general rule of theft: when a guest steals from the host, the hand is not cut. The source presents a clear ruling supported by an athar from az-Zuhri and a key legal reasoning about the concept of hirz (secure place). Understanding this exception deepens your appreciation of how the Hanafi school tailors penalties to the unique social context of hospitality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a guest stealing from the host. The ruling is that the hadd penalty of hand-cutting is not applied. Note that this is a negative ruling (no cutting), not a positive obligation.
The ruling is specific to the guest-host relationship.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is an athar (narration from a Companion or Successor) from az-Zuhri. He did not consider cutting for a man who was hosted and then betrayed them. This athar is from Musannaf Abdurrazzaq.
This dalil is of weak transmission (zanni al-thubut) but clear meaning (qati al-dalala) in the Hanafi view.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit statement 'lam yara 'alayhi qat'an' directly indicates the absence of cutting. The Hanafi school also provides a rational basis: the host's house is not a hirz for the guest because the guest is trusted with access and items become mubah (permissible) to some extent.
The concept of hirz is crucial: theft from a hirz is required for hadd.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
While the Hanafi school adheres to this ruling, other schools may consider the guest's theft as ordinary theft subject to hadd if other conditions are met. The difference arises from the definition of hirz and the extent of permission granted to the guest.
The ikhtilaf is a legitimate difference based on varying interpretations of hirz.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) and considered zahir al-riwayah. The reasoning that the guest's access nullifies the hirz is strong in the Hanafi usul framework.
No later tarjih needed; the ruling is consistent across Hanafi sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۴۶ (۲۴) ولا قطع على الضيف اذا سرق ممن اضافه 
نہیں کاٹنا ہے مہمان پر اگر وہ چرائے اس کی جس نے میزبانی کی ہو۔
مہمان نے میزبان کی چیز چرالی تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
اثر میں ہے۔ سئل الزهرى عن رجل ضاف قوما فاختانهم فلم ير عليه قطعا (الف) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الخيانة ج عاشرص ۲۱۰ نمبر ۱۸۸۶۵) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ مہمان میزبان کے گھر سے چرائے تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا (۲) مہمان کے لئے میزبان کا گھر حرز نہیں رہا۔ کیونکہ اس کے لئے گھر کا سامان ایک اندازے میں مباح ہو گیا۔]
There is no cutting for the guest if he steals from the one who hosted him. (Urdu: No cutting on the guest if he steals from his host. The guest stole the host's property, so the hand will not be cut. There is an athar: Az-Zuhri was asked about a man who was hosted by a people and he betrayed them, and he did not consider cutting on him. (Musannaf Abdurrazzaq, Bab al-Khiyanah, vol. 10, p. 210, no. 18865) From this athar, it is understood that if a guest steals from the host's house, the hand will not be cut. (2) For the guest, the host's house is not a hirs (secure place), because for him, the household items become permissible to some extent.)
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This is a ruling on theft by a guest. The passage states that if a guest steals from his host, the hand is not cut. The proof is an athar from az-Zuhri, who was asked about a man hosted by a people who betrayed them, and he did not see cutting. The reasoning is that the host's house is not considered a hirs (secure place) for the guest, because the guest is trusted and allowed access, so the items are not fully protected against him.

Key Terms
ضيف (dayf): A guest who is invited and hosted by someone.
حرز (hirz): A secure place that protects property from theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hand of a guest not cut when he steals from his host according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the guest is a family member.
B) Because the host's house is not a hirz for the guest.
C) Because the guest did not take the goods outside.
D) Because the guest's theft is not intentional.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts a guest from the hadd penalty for stealing from the host because the host's house is not a hirz for the guest.

Theft and Cutting: Accomplice vs. Retrieval
Ruling on two theft scenarios: goods passed to accomplice outside vs. thrown out and retrieved by same thief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting for two theft scenarios and explain the condition of personal removal from hirz.

Core Idea
Cutting requires personal removal of goods from the secure place; passing to an accomplice outside does not count.

This lesson examines two common theft scenarios: when a thief passes stolen goods to an accomplice waiting outside, and when the same thief throws the goods out and then retrieves them. The Hanafi school draws a sharp distinction based on whether the thief personally removed the goods from the secure location (hirz). Understanding this principle is crucial for applying the harsh penalty of hand-cutting correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage carefully. The first scenario involves a thief who enters, takes goods, and hands them to another person outside. The second scenario involves a thief who throws the goods out of the house and then himself retrieves them.
These are the two cases discussed.
2
State the ruling for each
For the first scenario, the ruling is that neither thief's hand is cut. For the second scenario, the ruling is that the thief's hand is cut.
The ruling is based on the presence or absence of personal removal from hirz.
3
Understand the key condition
The essential condition for cutting is that the thief must personally remove the goods from the secure location (hirz). In the first scenario, the inside thief did not remove the goods himself, and the outside thief did not take them from the hirz. In the second scenario, the act of throwing out and then retrieving constitutes removal by the same thief.
This condition is derived from the athar of Uthman and ash-Sha'bi.
4
Analyse the proofs
The passage cites athar (traditions) from Uthman (ra) and ash-Sha'bi that cutting is applied only when the thief takes the goods out of the house. Also an athar from Zuhri for the second scenario.
These prove the condition of removal.
5
Apply to distinguish the scenarios
In the first scenario, the goods were not removed by the thief; they were passed outside. In the second, the thief's own action completed the removal. Therefore, cutting applies only in the second.
The distinction rests on whether the thief personally brought the goods out of the hirz.

[۲۵۴۷] (۲۵) اگر چور نے گھر میں نقب لگایا اور داخل ہوا اور مال لیا اور دوسرے کو دے دیا جو گھر سے باہر تھا تو کسی پر ہاتھ کاٹا نہیں ہے۔ اور اگر راستے پر ڈال دیا پھر گھر سے نکلا تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔
چور نے گھر میں سوراخ کیا جس کو نقب لگانا کہتے ہیں پھر اندر داخل ہو کر مال اٹھایا اور خود گھر سے باہر نہیں لایا بلکہ گھر سے باہر دوسرا چور تھا اس کو پھینک کر دیا وہ لیکر گیا تو نہ گھر میں داخل ہونے والے کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا اور نہ باہر سے اچکنے والے کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ چوری اس کو کہتے ہیں کہ گھر کے اندر جا کر خود مال ساتھ لیکر باہر آئے تب اس کو چوری کہتے ہیں۔ یہ خود مال ساتھ لیکر باہر نہیں آیا ہے بلکہ دوسرے کو پھینک کر دیا اور باہر والے نے اچک لیا اس لئے چوری کا معنی کسی میں نہیں پایا گیا اس لئے کسی کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا نہ داخل ہونے والے کا کہ مال ساتھ لیکر باہر نہیں آیا اور باہر والے کا کیونکہ وہ گھر کے اندر سے نہیں لایا بلکہ سڑک پر مال اٹھایا ہے جو غیر محفوظ جگہ ہے (۲) اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ ان عثمان قضى انه لاقطع عليه وان كان قد جمع المتاع فاراد ان يسرق حتى يحوله ويخرج به ـ دوسری روایت میں ہے۔ عن الشعبي قال لا يقطع السارق حتى يخرج بالمتاع من البيت (ب) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب السارق يوجد فى البيت ولم يخرج، ج عاشر ص ۱۹۷/۱۹۶، نمبر ۱۸۸۱۵/۱۸۸۱۰ر مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۱۵۰ في القوم ينقب عليهم فيستغيثون فيجدون قو ما يسرقون فيؤ خذون منهم ؟ ج خامس ، ص ۵۴۹، نمبر ۲۸۹۱) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ سامان ساتھ لیکر باہر آیا ہو تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ یہاں ساتھ لیکر باہر نہیں آیا اور دوسرے نے گھر کے اندر یعنی مقام محفوظ سے مال نہیں اٹھایا بلکہ سڑک پر سے اٹھایا اس لئے اس کا بھی ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
اور اگر گھر کے اندر والے نے سامان گھر سے باہر پھینکا پھر باہر نکل کر خود ہی اس سامان کو اٹھا کر چلا تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ اس مسئلے میں سڑک پر سے کسی دوسرے چور نے نہیں اٹھایا بلکہ اندر والے چور نے ہی اٹھایا ہے اس لئے یہی سمجھا جائے گا کہ سامان ساتھ لیکر گھر سے باہر آیا۔ کیونکہ گھر سے باہر پھینکنا اور سامان کا اٹھانا ایک ہی چور کا کام ہے۔ اور ایسا ہوتا ہے کہ گھر اونچا ہو اور سڑک نیچی ہو تو گھر سے سامان سڑک پر پھینکتے ہیں پھر خالی ہاتھ نیچے اترتے ہیں پھر سامان لیکر بھاگتے ہیں۔ اس لئے سامان ساتھ لیکر نکلنا سمجھا جائے گا اس لئے ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔
اثر میں ہے۔ عن الزهري قال اذا جمع المتاع فخرج به من البيت الى الدار فعليه القطع (الف) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب السارق يوجد فى البيت ولم يخرج ج عاشر ص ۱۹۷ نمبر ۱۸۸۱۴/ مصنف ابن ابی هیته ۱۴۹ فی الرجل يسرق فطرح سرقته خارجا و يوخذ في البيت ما علیه؟ ج خاص ص ۵۴۹ نمبر ۲۸۹۱۰) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ سامان گھر سے باہر نکالا ہو تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔
سامان ساتھ لیکر گھر سے باہر آیا ہو تو اس کو چوری کہتے ہیں۔ دوسرے کو پھینک کر دیا تو چوری نہیں کہتے۔]
(25) If the thief made a breach in the house, entered, took the goods, and passed them to another who was outside the house, then there is no cutting for either. And if he threw it onto the path then came out, then cutting would be applied. The thief made a hole ( called naqb), then entered, picked up the goods, but did not bring them out himself; rather there was another thief outside, he threw it to him and he took it, so neither the one who entered nor the one who snatched from outside will have his hand cut. This issue is on the principle that theft is when one goes inside the house and personally brings the goods out with him, then it is called theft. Here he did not bring the goods out with him, but passed it to another, and the one outside snatched it, so the meaning of theft is not found in either, therefore no hand will be cut. There is an athar in support: Uthman (radiyallahu anhu) judged that there is no cutting even if he had gathered the goods and intended to steal until he moves it and takes it out. Another narration: from ash-Sha'bi, he said: The thief's hand is not cut until he takes the goods out of the house. (Musannaf Abdurrazzaq, Bab as-Sariq yujad fi al-bayt wa lam yakhruj, vol. 10, p. 196-197, nos. 18815/18810; Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, vol. 5, p. 549, no. 2891) From these athar it is understood that if the goods were taken out with him, then the hand is cut. Here he did not bring them out with him, and the other did not take the goods from inside the house (the secure place) but from the road, so his hand will not be cut either. And if the one inside threw the goods out of the house, then came out and picked it up himself and left, then the hand will be cut. In this case, no other thief on the road took it, but the inside thief himself picked it up, so it will be understood that he brought the goods out with him. Because throwing out of the house and then picking it up is the same thief's action. And it happens that the house is high and the path below, so they throw the goods onto the path from the house, then come down empty-handed, then pick up the goods and flee. Therefore it is considered taking the goods out with him, so the hand will be cut. There is an athar: from az-Zuhri, he said: If he gathered the goods and went out with them from the house to the courtyard, then cutting is upon him. (Musannaf Abdurrazzaq, Bab as-Sariq yujad fi al-bayt wa lam yakhruj, vol. 10, p. 197, no. 18814; Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, vol. 5, p. 549, no. 2891?) From this athar it is understood that if the goods were taken out of the house, the hand is cut. Bringing goods out of the house with him is called theft; passing them to someone else is not called theft.)
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage discusses two scenarios: (1) A thief enters a house, takes goods, and passes them to an accomplice outside. The Hanafi ruling is that neither thief's hand is cut. The reasoning is that for cutting to be wajib, the thief must personally remove the goods from the hirz (secure place). The one inside did not take it out; the one outside did not take it from inside the hirz (since he picked it up from outside). (2) If the same thief throws the goods out and then retrieves them himself, then cutting applies because he effectively removed them from the hirz. The proofs are athar from Uthman (ra) and ash-Sha'bi supporting the requirement of removing from the house.

Key Terms
نقب (naqb): Making a hole in a wall to steal.
ناول (nawala): To pass/hand over something to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling if the same thief who enters throws the goods out of the house and then retrieves them?
A) No cutting, because the goods were thrown out.
B) Cutting applies, because the thief effectively removed the goods from the house.
C) Cutting applies only to the accomplice.
D) No cutting, because the goods were outside the hirz when retrieved.

Key Takeaway
Cutting for theft requires the thief's personal removal of goods from the secure location; passing to an accomplice outside does not satisfy this condition.

Cutting Applies When Thief Uses a Donkey to Remove Goods
Ruling on theft using a donkey to remove goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft using an animal and explain why it is considered removal from hirz.

Core Idea
Using an animal to remove stolen goods is equivalent to carrying them out personally.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in theft (sariqa): a thief enters a house with a donkey, loads stolen goods onto it, and drives it out. The key question is whether this counts as 'removal' (ikhraj) from the protected place (hirz) so that the hadd cutting of the hand is applied. The source passage clarifies that it does, based on the principle that the thief's control over the animal makes the removal his own act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is theft using a donkey to transport goods out of the hirz. The ruling is that the thief's hand must be cut (wajib).
2
Understand the condition for hadd
For the hadd of cutting to apply, the goods must be removed from hirz by the thief or his agent. The passage emphasizes that removal is not limited to physical carrying on one's person.
The condition is ikhraj (taking out) from hirz.
3
Examine the qiyas (analogy)
The scenario is analogized to the standard case where the thief carries goods on his shoulder. Loading onto a donkey and driving it out is considered equivalent because people commonly use animals for heavy loads.
Qiyas: the effective cause (illa) is the thief's act of bringing the goods out under his control.
4
Apply the principle to the facts
Since the thief loaded the goods onto the donkey and led it out, he is the direct cause of removal. The animal acts as an instrument under his command.
The ruling is the same as if he carried the goods himself.
5
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, the hand is cut. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi position that any means of removal controlled by the thief suffices for the hadd.
Some other schools may require direct physical contact, but Hanafi relies on the concept of direct causation.

Source Text

(26) Similarly, the hand will be cut if he loaded the goods onto a donkey and drove it and took it out. The thief went inside the house and also took the donkey in, then loaded the goods onto the donkey and drove it out of the house; in this case too the hand will be cut. In this situation he did not carry the goods on his own shoulder, but loading onto the donkey and bringing it is also considered bringing with him. Because people bring heavy goods on donkeys. So it is as if he carried the goods on his shoulder and brought them out, therefore the hand will be cut. Loading onto an animal is also like loading onto one's own shoulder, and he brings it with him. This issue is derived from this principle. Tasuq: drives.)
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage clarifies that if a thief uses an animal like a donkey to remove stolen goods from the hirz, cutting still applies. Even if he does not physically carry the goods himself, directing the animal to carry them out is considered removal by him. The commentary explains that this is equivalent to carrying on his shoulder because that is how heavy items are typically transported. The ruling is an extension of the principle that the thief must personally bring the goods out; using an animal as a means counts as personal removal.

Key Terms
حمار (himar): Donkey used as a means of transport for stolen goods.
ساق (saqa): To drive (an animal) from behind, to lead out.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hand is cut because it is considered removal from hirz.
B) The hand is not cut because he did not carry the goods personally.
C) The hand is cut only if the donkey is also stolen.
D) The hand is not cut because the donkey is a separate agent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, any means by which the thief removes goods from the hirz under his control, including using an animal, triggers the hadd cutting.

Group Theft: Liability of Assistants and Lookouts
Ruling on group theft where only some take the goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on group theft where some members assist without directly taking goods, and apply the principle that full assistance renders one equally liable.

Core Idea
In group theft, all members are cut if they enter together and the total meets the nisab per head, even if only some take goods.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: a group enters a secure place to steal, but only a few actually pick up the goods while others act as lookouts or guards. The Hanafi school rules that all members of the group are subject to the hadd punishment of hand cutting. Why? Because the assistants are considered full participants in the removal of goods from the secure place. Their cooperation is essential to the theft, so they are legally treated as direct takers. We will explore the conditions and the underlying principle of 'complete assistance'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the act
The group enters a secure place (hirz) with intent to steal. Some take goods, others assist by watching or guarding. The ruling is that all are cut.
2
Note the condition of nisab
The total stolen goods must be such that each person's share, if divided equally, exceeds ten dirhams (the nisab for theft).
If the share is exactly ten or less, hadd is not applied.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The assistants are considered co-participants because their aid is indispensable. Theft in a group context often requires lookouts; they are like direct takers.
The principle is 'full assistance in theft makes one like a direct taker'.
4
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools may require each thief to personally remove goods to be liable. The Hanafi position is more inclusive, based on collective criminal liability.
5
Apply the principle to variations
Consider cases where some enter without intent, or where the nisab is not met. This lesson focuses on the core ruling.

Source Text

(27) If a group entered a secure place (hirz) and some of them took the goods, then all of them will have their hands cut. For example, a group of five people entered a secure place for theft. Among them, three took the goods, and the rest kept watch so that no one would come and catch them. And they stole enough that each one's share is more than ten dirhams, then all will have their hands cut. In a group, it happens that some pick up the goods and the others keep an eye on the residents so that no one comes to catch them. With their help, the ones picking up goods do so, so it is as if all participated in taking the goods from the secure place and bringing them out, therefore all will have their hands cut. This issue is on the principle that the one who gives full assistance in theft is like the one who takes the goods and brings them out.)
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage states that when a group enters a secure place (hirz) with the intent to steal, and only some members actually take the goods while others act as lookouts or assist, all members of the group are subject to hadd cutting. The reasoning is that the assistants are considered co-participants in the removal of goods from the hirz because their support enables the theft. The condition is that the total stolen goods must meet the nisab (ten dirhams) per person or overall? The commentary says each one's share is more than ten dirhams, likely meaning the total is sufficient that each would get at least that if divided. The principle is that full assistance in theft renders one equally liable.

Key Terms
جماعة (jama'ah): A group of people acting together with a common purpose.
تولى (tawalla): To take charge; here it means some undertook the actual taking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) All four are cut only if the total exceeds 40 dirhams.
D) None are cut because the taker's share is 50, but the watchers share is 0.

Key Takeaway
In group theft, every member who enters the hirz with common intent and provides indispensable assistance is liable for hadd cutting, provided each one's share of the total stolen exceeds the nisab of ten dirhams.

Completion of Theft by Removal from Hiraz
Explains when theft is complete by removal from secure place

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that removal from hiraz by any means (throwing, loading on an animal) completes the theft, triggering the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Removal from the secure place (hiraz) completes theft, even without physical carrying.

In the Hanafi school, the hadd punishment for theft requires that the stolen goods be removed from their secure place (hiraz). But what exactly counts as removal? This lesson explores two scenarios where the thief does not physically carry the goods out: throwing them out of a high house and then retrieving them, and loading them on a donkey and leading it out. The Hanafi position, supported by an athar from al-Zuhri, is that any act that takes the goods out of the hiraz suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two cases: (1) throwing goods from a high house onto the road, then going out to pick them up; (2) loading goods on a donkey inside the house and driving it out.
Both involve indirect removal
2
Extract the ruling for each
In both cases, the hand is cut. The common element is that the goods leave the hiraz by the thief's action.
Ruling: cut (hadd)
3
Analyze the proof
The author cites an athar from al-Zuhri: 'When he collects the goods and goes out with them from the room to the courtyard, then cutting is upon him.' This shows removal within the same property triggers cutting, so removal outside also triggers it.
Proof: mursal hadith
4
Understand the rationale
The commentary explains that throwing or using an animal is equivalent to carrying out oneself. The key is that the goods leave the hiraz due to the thief's deliberate act.
Rationale: removal is complete

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He threw it on the road then went out and took it: cutting [is applied]. Similarly, if he loaded it on a donkey and drove it and took it out. [Explanation:] He came out of the house. Because throwing out of the house and taking the goods is the act of one thief. And it happens that the house is high and the road low, so they throw goods from the house to the road, then come down empty-handed, then take the goods and flee. Therefore, it is considered as taking the goods with him, so his hand is cut. There is an athar: from al-Zuhri, he said: 'When he collects the goods and goes out with them from the room to the courtyard, then cutting is upon him.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-sariq yujad fi al-bayt wa lam yakhruj, vol. 10 p. 197 no. 18814; Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, Fi al-rajul yasriq fa yatrah sariqatahu kharijan wa yu'khadh fi al-bayt ma 'alayh?, vol. 6 p. 549 no. 28910). From this athar it is known that if goods are taken out of the house, the hand is cut. If he comes out of the house with the goods, it is called theft; if he throws them to another, it is not called theft. Naqb: making a hole in the house to take out goods. [2548] (26) Similarly, the hand will be cut if he loads goods on a donkey and drives it and takes it out. The thief went inside the house and took the donkey with him, then loaded goods on the donkey and drove it out of the house; still the hand is cut. In this case, he did not carry the goods on his shoulder himself, but loading on the donkey is also taking with him. Because people load heavy goods on donkeys. Therefore, it is as if he carried the goods on his shoulder and brought them out, so his hand is cut. Loading on any animal is like loading on one's own shoulder and bringing it out. This issue is derived from this principle.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on the completion of theft by removal from a secure place (hiraz). The matn states two scenarios: (1) the thief throws goods out of a high house onto the road and then goes out to pick them up, and (2) the thief loads goods on a donkey and leads it out. In both cases, the hand is cut. The commentary explains that any removal from the hiraz, whether by throwing, carrying, or using an animal, constitutes takhrij (taking out) which is a condition for the hadd of theft. The proof is an athar from Imam al-Zuhri that if goods are collected and taken from the room to the courtyard, cutting applies. The commentary clarifies that if the thief throws goods to another person outside, it is not considered theft by the thrower because there is no simultaneous removal by him; the receiver might be liable. Also, the term 'naqb' is defined as making a hole, which is treated separately later.

Key Terms
حرز (hiraz): A secure place where goods are stored, such as a house or shop.
قطع (qat'): Cutting of the hand as a hadd punishment for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a thief loads stolen goods onto a donkey inside the house and leads the donkey out, according to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) No cutting because he did not carry the goods himself
B) Cutting because the goods are removed from hiraz
C) Only ta'zir punishment
D) Cutting only if the goods are heavy

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, theft is complete when the goods are removed from their secure place by any deliberate act of the thief, whether by throwing, carrying, or using an animal.

Group Theft: Liability of All Participants
Explains collective liability for theft from a secure place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on group theft and explain why all accomplices, including guards, are subject to cutting.

Core Idea
Every participant in theft, even if only assisting, is cut if their share exceeds the nisab.

Theft from a secure place (hiraz) triggers the hadd punishment of hand-cutting, but when multiple people are involved, who exactly is liable? This passage clarifies that in the Hanafi school, anyone who contributes—whether by taking goods, keeping watch, or otherwise assisting—is considered a participant and faces the same penalty, provided each person's portion meets the nisab threshold. This principle extends the notion of direct taking to include full assistance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Name the act (group theft from hiraz) and the ruling (hadd cutting for all). The commentary confirms that all members of the group are cut.
2
List the Conditions
Determine what must be present for the hadd to apply: entry into hiraz, group activity, some members physically taking the goods, others assisting (e.g., keeping watch), and each person's share of the stolen property reaching the nisab of 10 dirhams.
The passage illustrates with five thieves: three take, two guard.
3
Understand the Role of Assistants
Recognise that guards and lookouts are considered participants because they enable the taking. Their assistance is deemed equivalent to direct removal in Hanafi law.
The commentary states: 'one who provides full assistance in theft is like the one who takes and brings out.'
4
Note the Underlying Principle
Extract the legal maxim: full assistance in theft is treated as direct participation. This is unique to the Hanafi school and contrasts with other madhabs that may require physical taking.
5
Consider the Scholarly Difference
Acknowledge that this ruling is mukhtalaf fih; some schools require each individual to directly remove the goods. The Hanafi position is based on broader participation.
The cross-madhhab status is marked as disputed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا دخل الحرز جماعة فتولى بعضهم الاخذ قطعوا جميعا [۲۵۴۹) (۲۷) [۲۵۴۹) (۲۷) اگر مکان محفوظ میں ایک جماعت داخل ہوئی اور بعض نے مال لیا تو سب کے ہاتھ کاٹے جائیں گے۔ مثلا پانچ آدمیوں کی جماعت مکان محفوظ میں چوری کے لئے داخل ہوئی۔ ان میں سے تین نے مال لیا اور باقی آنے والوں کی نگرانی کرتے رہے کہ کوئی آکر پکڑ نہ لے۔ اور اتنامال چرایا کہ ہر ایک کو دس دس درہم سے زیادہ ملے تو سب کے ہاتھ کاٹے جائیں گے۔ جماعت میں ایسا ہی ہوتا ہے کہ بعض مال اٹھاتا ہے اور باقی گھر والوں پر نظر رکھتے ہیں کہ کوئی آکر پکڑ نہ لے۔ ان کی مدد سے ہی مال اٹھانے والے مال اٹھاتے ہیں تو گویا کہ مکان محفوظ سے مال اٹھا کر ساتھ لانے میں سب شریک ہوئے اس لئے سب کے ہاتھ کاٹے جائیں گے۔ یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ چوری میں پوری مدد کرنے والا بھی مال ہی اٹھانے والا اور ساتھ لیکر باہر آنے والا ہے۔
And if a group enters the hiraz and some of them take [the goods], all of them are cut. [2549] (27) If a group entered the secure house and some took the goods, then all their hands will be cut. For example, a group of five entered the secure house for theft. Among them, three took the goods and the others kept watch so that no one comes and catches them. And they stole such an amount that each gets more than ten dirhams, then all their hands will be cut. In a group, it is like that: some take the goods and the others keep an eye on the house owners so that no one catches them. With their help, the takers take the goods, so it is as if they all participated in taking the goods out of the secure house and bringing them out, therefore all their hands will be cut. This issue is based on the principle that one who provides full assistance in theft is like the one who takes and brings out.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This is a ruling on group theft. The matn states that if a group enters a secure place and some take the goods, all members are subject to cutting. The commentary elaborates using an example: five thieves enter; three take goods, two keep watch. If the total stolen gives each a share exceeding the nisab (10 dirhams), all five have their hands cut. The principle is that those who guard or assist are participants in the removal, thus liable for the hadd. This is a direct ruling from the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
حرز (hiraz): A secure place (e.g., locked store, vault) from which theft may occur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, who among a group of thieves is subject to hadd cutting?
A) Only those who physically pick up the goods
B) Only those who directly remove goods from the hiraz
C) All members of the group, including guards, if each share exceeds nisab
D) All members of the group regardless of share

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, any accomplice who assists in theft from a secure place is considered a direct participant and liable for hadd, provided their share of the loot meets the nisab.

Theft by Making a Hole in the Wall: Hanafi Ruling and Shafi'i Dispute
States that inserting hand through a hole does not warrant cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft via a hole, explain why hand insertion alone does not warrant cutting, and compare it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Theft that warrants hand-cutting requires the thief's entire body to enter the hiraz; inserting a hand is insufficient.

Theft is a serious crime with a fixed punishment in Islamic law. However, the Hanafi school specifies that the hadd of theft applies only when the thief fully enters the secure place. This lesson examines a case where a thief makes a hole and inserts his hand without entering, and why this does not trigger the cutting punishment, as well as the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the source: no cutting of the hand when the thief inserts his hand through a hole without entering the building.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognise that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees: he requires cutting because the hand entered a secure place and took goods.
The Shafi'i school focuses on the removal of goods from hiraz, not the method of entry.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
Analyse the athar from Caliph Ali: he did not cut the hand of a man who made a hole but gave ta'zir. Understand how this is used as a proof (dalil naqli).
The athar is from Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, considered reliable by Hanafis.
4
Understand the Hanafi response to the opponent
The Hanafi school argues that theft (sariqa) legally occurs only when the thief enters the hiraz with his whole body and removes the goods. Mere hand insertion is not full theft.
This is based on the concept of hiraz and the requirement of full entry.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, a thief who uses a hole without entering escapes the hadd under Hanafi but would be cut under Shafi'i. This affects sentencing.
The Hanafi position is more lenient in this specific case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن نقب البيت وادخل يده فيه واخذ شيئا لم يقطع [۲۵۵۰] (۲۸) کسی نے کمرے میں نقب ڈالا اور اس میں ہاتھ داخل کیا اور کچھ لیا تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ کسی نے کمرے میں سوراخ کر کے ہاتھ ڈالا خود داخل نہیں ہوا اور اندر سے کچھ نکال لیا تو ہاتھ نہیں کا ٹا جائے گا۔ کمرہ خود حرز ہے اس سے چرانے کا طریقہ یہ ہے کہ خود آدمی کمرے میں داخل ہو اور وہاں سے ساتھ سامان لائے تب چوری ہوگی۔ اور یہاں خود کمرے میں داخل نہیں ہوا بلکہ ہاتھ ڈال کر نکالا ہے اس لئے چوری نہیں پائی گئی اس لئے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ اتي على برجل نقب بيتا فلم يقطعه وعزره اسواطا (الف) (مصنف عبد الرزاق، باب فی الرجل يلقب البيت و يوخذ منه المتاع ج عاشرص ۱۹۹ نمبر ۱۸۸۲۱) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ اس طرح سے نقب لگانے سے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ مقام محفوظ سے ہاتھ ڈال کر سامان چرایا ہے۔ چاہے کمرے میں داخل نہیں ہوا اس لئے ہاتھ کا ٹا جائے گا۔
And whoever makes a hole in the house and inserts his hand and takes something, he is not cut. [2550] (28) Someone made a hole in the room and inserted his hand and took something, then his hand will not be cut. Someone made a hole in the room and inserted his hand; he himself did not enter and took something out, then his hand will not be cut. The room itself is a hiraz; the method of stealing from it is that the person himself enters the room and brings goods from there, then theft occurs. Here, he did not enter the room but inserted his hand and took out, so theft is not established, therefore his hand is not cut. There is an athar: 'Ali was brought a man who had made a hole in a house, and he did not cut his hand but gave him ta'zir with lashes.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab fi al-rajul yalqab al-bayt wa yu'khadh minhu al-mata', vol. 10 p. 199 no. 18821) From this athar, it is known that by making a hole in this way, the hand is not cut. Imam al-Shafi'i says: He stole from a secure place by inserting his hand, even if he did not enter the room, so his hand should be cut.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling and a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. The matn states that if a thief makes a hole in the wall and inserts his hand to take goods without entering the house, his hand is not cut. The commentary provides the reasoning: theft requires the thief's entire body to enter the hiraz and remove the goods; merely inserting a hand is insufficient. The proof is an athar from Caliph 'Ali who did not cut the hand of a man who made a hole but gave him ta'zir instead. Imam al-Shafi'i opposes: since the hand entered the secure place and took goods, cutting is required. The Hanafi position distinguishes between entering the hiraz (complete theft) and mere insertion (incomplete).

Key Terms
نقب (naqb): Making a hole in a wall to steal.
حرز (hiraz): A secure place where goods are kept.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment less than hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a thief makes a hole in a wall and inserts his hand to take goods without entering, what is the ruling?
A) Hand is cut
B) No cutting, only ta'zir
C) Cutting if goods exceed nisab
D) He is executed

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the hadd of theft is only applied when the thief physically enters the hiraz; mere insertion of a hand through a hole does not warrant cutting.

Ruling on Theft from a Box or Sleeve
Explains that inserting hand into a box or sleeve warrants cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a thief inserts his hand into a secure container and identify the condition of hiraz that triggers cutting.

Core Idea
Hand entry into a hiraz constitutes theft warranting cutting even without full body entry.

This lesson examines a specific application of the theft hadd: when the thief inserts only his hand into a container that cannot be entered bodily. The Hanafi school holds that such action is sufficient for cutting, provided the container is a secure place (hiraz). The ruling clarifies that outer pockets or open containers do not qualify.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act is inserting the hand into a money-changer's box or another person's sleeve (inner pocket) and taking money.
The sleeve pocket is a traditional secure place (hiraz).
2
Determine if the container is a hiraz
A hiraz is a secure place that cannot be entered bodily. The box and inner sleeve pocket are hiraz. An outer pocket is not.
The distinction is based on customary security.
3
Apply the ruling
If the container is a hiraz, the thief's hand is cut. If not, no cutting.
The ruling is specific to theft from hiraz; other conditions of hadd (nisab, etc.) are assumed.
4
Understand the rationale
Since the container cannot be entered bodily, hand entry is the equivalent of entry into a secure place. This fulfills the requirement of theft from hiraz.
The rationale is based on the impossibility of full body entry, not on the thief's intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ادخل يده في صندوق الصيرفي او في كم غيره واخذ المال قُطع [۲۵۵۱] (۲۹) اگر ہاتھ ڈالا سنار کے صندوق میں یا دوسرے کی جیب میں اور مال لیا تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ صندوق میں یا جیب میں آدمی داخل نہیں ہو سکتا بلکہ ایک ہی طریقہ ہے کہ ہاتھ ڈال کر نکالے۔ اس لئے ہاتھ ڈال کر نکال تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ مقام محفوظ سے چوری پائی گئی۔ افت میر فنی : صراف سے مشتق ہے سناریا جو نوٹ بھتا ہو، کم : آستین، اہل عرب آستین میں جیب بناتے تھے اس لئے کم کہہ دیا۔ یہاں مراد ہے آستین کے اندر کی جیب جو حرز ہے اور محفوظ ہے۔ اس لئے اگر آستین کے باہر جیب ہو اور اس کو کاٹ کر درہم لے لے تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا کیونکہ باہر کی جیب حرز نہیں ہے۔
And if he inserts his hand into a money-changer's box or into another's sleeve and takes the money, he is cut. [2551] (29) If he puts his hand into a money-changer's box or into another's pocket and takes the money, his hand will be cut. A person cannot enter a box or pocket; the only way is to insert the hand and take out. Therefore, if he inserts his hand and takes out, his hand is cut because theft from a secure place has occurred. 'Sairfi' is derived from 'sarraf' meaning money-changer; 'kumm' means sleeve, the Arabs used to make pockets in sleeves, so they called it 'kumm'. Here it means the pocket inside the sleeve which is a secure place. Therefore, if there is a pocket outside the sleeve and he cuts it and takes dirhams, his hand is not cut because the outer pocket is not a secure place.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This ruling addresses theft from small containers or pockets that cannot be entered physically. The matn states that inserting a hand into a money-changer's box or another person's sleeve (pocket) and taking money results in cutting. The commentary explains that because it is impossible to enter such places bodily, the act of inserting the hand to take goods is sufficient for theft. However, if the pocket is on the outside of the garment and not considered a secure place (hiraz), cutting does not apply. This shows that the condition of 'entry into hiraz' is interpreted according to common practice: a closed box or inner pocket is hiraz, but an open outer pocket is not.

Key Terms
صندوق الصيرفي (sunduq al-sayrafi): A money-changer's box or chest where money is kept.
كم (kumm): Sleeve, which historically contained an inner pocket considered a secure place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A thief inserts his hand into a money-changer's box and takes coins. Under Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling?
A) No cutting because he did not enter the box
B) Cutting because the box is a secure place and he removed goods
C) Only ta'zir
D) Cutting only if the box is open

Key Takeaway
Inserting hand into a secure container that cannot be entered bodily constitutes theft warranting cutting in Hanafi law.
